So altitude from M is line through origin with direction (√15, -1). Its equation: (x,y) = t(√15, -1). So points: y = -(1/√15)x? Actually slope = -1/√15. So line: y = - (1/√15) x.

Now intersection with x = 5/4 gives y = - (1/√15)*(5/4) = -5/(4√15). So H = (5/4, -5/(4√15)). That is different from our previous H (which was -√15/4). So our previous H calculation was wrong because we incorrectly computed altitude from M. Let's recalc: We previously computed altitude from M to PN: we used slope PN = (0 - y_P)/(d - x_P) = (-(-√15/4))/(3/2 - 5/4) = (√15/4)/(1/4) = √15. So slope of PN = √15. That's correct because PN = (1/4, √15/4) so slope = (√15/4)/(1/4)=√15. So altitude from M is perpendicular, slope = -1/√15. Equation: y = (-1/√15) x. Intersection with x=5/4 gives y = -5/(4√15). So H = (5/4, -5/(4√15)). Now note that P = (5/4, -√15/4) = (5/4, - (√15)/4). Since √15/4 ≈0.9682, and 5/(4√15) = 5/(4*3.87298)=5/15.4919=0.3228. So H = (1.25, -0.3228). That matches our earlier H? Earlier we got H=(1.25,-0.3228). Yes! Because we computed H as (1.25, -0.3228). And we computed y_H = x_P(d-x_P)/y_P = 1.25*(1.5-1.25)/(-0.9682)=1.25*0.25/(-0.9682)=0.3125/(-0.9682)=-0.3228. So that's the same as -5/(4√15) because 5/(4√15)=5/(4*3.87298)=5/15.4919=0.3228. So H = (5/4, -5/(4√15)). So indeed H is not equal to P; we mistakenly thought P = (5/4, -√15/4) and H = (5/4, -√15/4) but that was wrong because -√15/4 = -0.9682, not -0.3228. So earlier I mis-typed: y_P = -√15/4, so P = (5/4, -0.9682). H = (5/4, -0.3228). So they are distinct. Good. So H is correct.

Now line through H parallel to AP: AP direction v = (3/2, -√15/2). So L: (5/4, -5/(4√15)) + t(3/2, -√15/2). We need to check if L is tangent to circle (BEF). Let's compute again with exact values.

We have:

H = (5/4, -5/(4√15)). v = (3/2, -√15/2). So L param: x = 5/4 + (3/2)t, y = -5/(4√15) - (√15/2)t.

Simplify: Write in terms of rational and √15. Let's compute line equation.

We have direction v. Normal vector n = (√15/2, 3/2) (since dot (3/2, -√15/2)·(√15/2, 3/2)= (3/2)*(√15/2)+(-√15/2)*(3/2)= (3√15)/4 - (3√15)/4=0). So line L: n·(X - H)=0 => (√15/2)(x - 5/4) + (3/2)(y + 5/(4√15)) = 0. Multiply by 2: √15(x - 5/4) + 3(y + 5/(4√15)) = 0 => √15 x - (5√15)/4 + 3y + (15)/(4√15) = 0. Simplify 15/(4√15)= (15√15)/(4*15)= √15/4. So equation: √15 x + 3y - (5√15)/4 + √15/4 = 0 => √15 x + 3y - (4√15)/4 = 0 => √15 x + 3y - √15 = 0 => √15 x + 3y = √15. So line L: √15 x + 3y = √15. (Equation L)

Now check H: H=(5/4, -5/(4√15)). LHS = √15*(5/4) + 3*(-5/(4√15)) = (5√15)/4 - (15)/(4√15) = (5√15)/4 - (15√15)/(4*15)= (5√15)/4 - (√15)/4 = (4√15)/4 = √15. So H lies on L, good.

Now we need to show L is tangent to circumcircle of BEF.

We have O1 = (3/4, -9√15/20). Compute distance from O1 to L.

Line L: √15 x + 3y - √15 = 0. Distance d = |√15*(3/4) + 3*(-9√15/20) - √15| / sqrt((√15)^2+3^2) = | (3√15)/4 - (27√15)/20 - √15 | / sqrt(15+9) = | (3√15)/4 - (27√15)/20 - √15 | / √24 = | √15(3/4 - 27/20 - 1) | / (2√6) because √24=2√6.

Compute coefficient: 3/4 = 15/20, so 3/4 - 27/20 = -12/20 = -3/5. Then -1 = -5/5, so total = -3/5 - 5/5 = -8/5. So absolute = | -8√15/5 | = 8√15/5. Then d = (8√15/5) / (2√6) = (8√15)/(10√6) = (4√15)/(5√6) = (4/5) √(15/6) = (4/5) √(5/2) = (4/5) * √10/2 = (2√10)/5? Actually √(5/2)=√10/2, so (4/5)*(√10/2)= (2√10)/5. So d = 2√10/5.

Now radius R1 = 2√10/5. So d = R1. Thus distance from O1 to L equals radius, so L is tangent to circle (BEF). Great! So the claim holds for this numeric example. Good.

Thus our earlier mistake was using wrong H (coinciding with P). Now we have correct H and it works. So the proof is plausible.

Now we need to provide a rigorous synthetic proof. The analytic approach can be turned into a proof by coordinate geometry, but we need to justify that the line through H parallel to AP is tangent to circle (BEF). Since we have explicit expressions, we can compute the distance and show equality. However we must handle general a, b, d (with a<b, and circles intersect). But we can set up coordinate system as we did: M=(0,0), N=(d,0) with d>0. Then define a = radius of Ω, b = radius of Γ, with a<b. The circles intersect, so we have |a-b|<d<a+b, but also a<b implies d > b-a. Also, the common chord AB is perpendicular to MN at O, with x-coordinate x0 = (d^2 + a^2 - b^2)/(2d). Then A = (x0, y0) with y0 = sqrt(a^2 - x0^2). B = (x0, -y0). C = (-a, 0). D = (d+b, 0). Then P is circumcenter of triangle ACD. We can compute coordinates of P exactly as we did: x_P = (d+b-a)/2, y_P = -x_P (x0+a)/y0. And we can compute t_E = a(d+a+b)/|v|^2, t_F = b(d+a+b)/|v|^2, where v = P-A. And we can compute H as orthocenter of triangle PMN: Since M=(0,0), N=(d,0), P=(x_P, y_P). The altitude from P is vertical line x=x_P. Altitude from M is line through M perpendicular to PN. So H = (x_P, y_H) where y_H is determined by the intersection. We can compute y_H as? Using the formula for orthocenter: For triangle with vertices (0,0), (d,0), (x_P, y_P). The orthocenter H is given by (x_P, - (x_P (d - x_P))/y_P )? Actually from our derivation: altitude from M: line through M perpendicular to PN. Vector PN = (d - x_P, -y_P). So a perpendicular direction is (y_P, d - x_P) or ( -y_P, d - x_P )? Dot (d-x_P, -y_P)·(y_P, d-x_P)= (d-x_P)y_P + (-y_P)(d-x_P)=0. So altitude from M: y = ((d-x_P)/y_P) x? Wait, line through origin with direction (y_P, d-x_P) has slope = (d-x_P)/y_P. But we need perpendicular to PN. Since PN has slope = -y_P/(d-x_P). So perpendicular slope = (d-x_P)/y_P. So altitude from M: y = ((d-x_P)/y_P) x. Intersection with x = x_P gives y_H = ((d-x_P)/y_P) x_P. So H = (x_P, x_P(d-x_P)/y_P). Since y_P is negative, y_H is negative? But in our numeric, y_P = -√15/4, so x_P(d-x_P)/y_P = (5/4)*(1.5-1.25)/(-√15/4)= (5/4*0.25)/(-0.9682)=0.3125/(-0.9682) = -0.3228, which matches. So H = (x_P, x_P(d-x_P)/y_P). Note that y_P is negative, so y_H negative. Good.

Now we need to show that line through H parallel to AP is tangent to circle (BEF). We can compute using analytic geometry.

We have A, B, E, F. We already derived that E = A + t_E v, F = A + t_F v, with t_E = a(d+a+b)/|v|^2, t_F = b(d+a+b)/|v|^2, and v = P-A. Also we have |v|^2 =? We can compute |v|^2 in terms of known quantities. From earlier, we found A·v = -a(d+a+b)/2, and (A-N)·v = -b(d+a+b)/2. Also we can compute |v|^2 = dx^2+dy^2. But we may not need explicit.

We can find the circumcircle of BEF. Since B is symmetric to A across x-axis, and E and F are on line AP. Maybe we can show that the line through H parallel to AP is the polar of some point with respect to circle (BEF). Alternatively, we can compute directly the distance from the circumcenter O1 of BEF to L, and show it equals the circumradius. This will involve algebraic manipulation. We can do it in general with variables a, b, d, and y0, x0, etc. Since we already have derived expressions in terms of a, b, d, and we can compute O1 and L. However the expressions may be messy but we can simplify using relationships.

We can also try to find a synthetic proof. Let's explore synthetic approach.

We have circles Ω (M, a) and Γ (N, b) intersect at A and B. Line MN meets Ω at C and Γ at D, with order C-M-N-D. P is circumcenter of ACD. AP meets Ω again at E and Γ again at F. H is orthocenter of triangle PMN.

Goal: line through H parallel to AP is tangent to circumcircle of BEF.

Observations:

- Since M and N are centers, AB is the common chord, and MN is the line of centers. O = midpoint of AB lies on MN, and MO ⟂ AB, NO ⟂ AB. So O is the foot of perpendicular from M and N to AB.

- Points C and D are on MN, with C on Ω on the left of M, D on Γ on the right of N. So C and D are the intersections of MN with the circles, but not necessarily the endpoints? Actually Ω: MN is a line through M, it meets Ω at two points: one on each side of M. Since M is between C and N, and N is to the right, the left intersection is C = (-a,0) in our coordinate. Similarly, Γ: MN meets Γ at two points: one on each side of N. Since N is between M and D, D is the right intersection = (d+b,0). So C and D are the farthest points on the line.

- Triangle ACD: A is intersection point, C on Ω, D on Γ. Its circumcenter P. Since C and D lie on MN, the perpendicular bisector of CD is perpendicular to MN. So P lies on that perpendicular bisector. Also, note that A is not on MN, so P is not on MN generally.

- Line AP: Since P is circumcenter of ACD, AP is a line from A to the circumcenter. In triangle ACD, the line from vertex A to circumcenter P is the perpendicular bisector of side CD? No, the perpendicular bisector of CD is perpendicular to MN, and P lies on it, but A is not necessarily on that line. So AP is not necessarily perpendicular to CD.

- We derived that t_E = a(d+a+b)/|v|^2, t_F = b(d+a+b)/|v|^2. So the points E and F are on the ray from A through P, with ratios t_E and t_F. So E and F are determined by the homothety from A? Actually we have AE:AP = a(d+a+b)/|v|^2? But |v|^2 = |AP|^2? No, v = P-A, so |v| = AP. So |v|^2 = AP^2. Then t_E = a(d+a+b)/AP^2, t_F = b(d+a+b)/AP^2. So AE = t_E * AP = a(d+a+b)/AP, and AF = b(d+a+b)/AP. So AE/AP = a/AP? Actually t_E = a(d+a+b)/AP^2, so AE = a(d+a+b)/AP. So AE/AP = a/AP? Not constant. But note that AP is the distance from A to circumcenter of ACD. There might be a relation with the power of A with respect to circle with diameter something.

- Also, we found A·v = -a(d+a+b)/2. Since v = P-A, this gives A·(P-A) = -a(d+a+b)/2 => A·P - |A|^2 = -a(d+a+b)/2. But |A|^2 = a^2 (since A on Ω). So A·P = a^2 - a(d+a+b)/2 = a(2a - (d+a+b))/2 = a(a - b - d)/2? Actually 2a - (d+a+b) = a - b - d. So A·P = a(a - b - d)/2. Since a<b, a-b-d negative. So A·P negative, meaning angle between A and P is >90°? Not important.

- Similarly, (A-N)·v = -b(d+a+b)/2.

- Now H = orthocenter of triangle PMN. Since M and N are on x-axis, altitude from P is vertical line x=x_P. Altitude from M is line through M perpendicular to PN. So H = (x_P, x_P(d-x_P)/y_P). In our coordinate, y_P is negative, so y_H negative.

Now line through H parallel to AP: direction v. So L: H + t v. We want L tangent to circle (BEF). Since B is symmetric to A across x-axis, and E,F lie on AP, maybe the circle (BEF) has some symmetry. Also note that B is the reflection of A across MN. So B is the mirror of A. The line AP is not symmetric; its reflection across MN is the line through B and the reflection of P (call P'). Since P is not on MN generally, its reflection P' is (x_P, -y_P). That line B P' might be related to something. Could be that the circle (BEF) is the image of circle (A something) under reflection. But E and F are on AP, their reflections across MN would be points on line B P'? Not exactly.

Another idea: Use inversion about A. Since A lies on both circles, inversion with center A and arbitrary radius may map the circles to lines. Under inversion, circles through A become lines not passing through A. So Ω (through A) becomes a line; Γ becomes another line. The line MN (not through A generally) becomes a circle through A? Actually inversion: a line not through A becomes a circle through A. So MN becomes a circle through A. Also points C and D lie on MN, so their images will lie on that circle. P is circumcenter of ACD; under inversion, what becomes? The circumcircle of ACD passes through A, so becomes a line. The circumcenter P (which is the center of that circle) does not have a simple image. Might be complicated.

Alternatively, use complex numbers with coordinates. Since we have a coordinate system that simplifies, we can carry out the proof analytically. That would be rigorous, though a bit computational. But we need to present a proof that is clear and justified. The problem is likely from an olympiad where synthetic solutions are expected, but analytic is also acceptable. However we must ensure that we do not rely on numerical evidence but derive algebraic identities.

Let's attempt an analytic proof. We'll set up coordinates as before. We'll derive expressions for all points in terms of a, b, d, and y0, x0. Then we'll compute the circumcircle of BEF, its center O1, and the distance from O1 to line L. We'll show that distance equals the radius. This will involve some algebra but we can use simplifications like the fact that x0^2+y0^2=a^2, and also (d-x0)^2+y0^2=b^2. And we have relationships from the fact that P is circumcenter of ACD, which gave us x_P = (d+b-a)/2, y_P = -x_P (x0+a)/y0. We can also compute AP^2 = |v|^2 = (x_P-x0)^2+(y_P-y0)^2. We already computed x0 dx + y0 dy = -a(d+a+b)/2, and (x0-d)dx + y0 dy = -b(d+a+b)/2. We also need |v|^2. We can compute |v|^2 = (dx^2+dy^2). We can find it from the fact that A, C, D are known, and P is circumcenter, so we can compute AP^2 = R^2 (circumradius of triangle ACD). But maybe we don't need explicit |v|^2; we can use that t_E = a(d+a+b)/|v|^2, etc. For the circle (BEF), we might compute its equation using the fact that B, E, F are known in terms of A and v.

Let's do the algebra in general.

Let’s denote:

M = (0,0), N = (d,0), with d>0.

Let A = (x0, y0) with y0>0, and x0^2+y0^2 = a^2, (d-x0)^2+y0^2 = b^2, and a<b. Also, since circles intersect, we have |a-b|<d<a+b, and since a<b, we have d > b-a. Also, note that x0 = (d^2 + a^2 - b^2)/(2d). This can be positive or negative; but we don't need to assume sign.

Let C = (-a, 0), D = (d+b, 0). (We should verify that these are the intersections with the line MN: For Ω, x^2+y^2=a^2, line y=0 gives x=±a. Since M is at 0 and N is at d>0, the left point is -a, right is a. But note that the order is C, M, N, D. That implies -a < 0 < d < d+b, so indeed C = (-a,0) and D = (d+b,0). Good.

Now P is circumcenter of triangle ACD. Since C and D lie on x-axis, the perpendicular bisector of CD is vertical line x = (x_C+x_D)/2 = (-a + (d+b))/2 = (d+b-a)/2. So x_P = (d+b-a)/2.

Now we need y_P. P also lies on perpendicular bisector of AC (or AD). Let's find using the condition that PA = PC. Since PC^2 = (x_P - (-a))^2 + (y_P - 0)^2 = (x_P + a)^2 + y_P^2. And PA^2 = (x_P - x0)^2 + (y_P - y0)^2. Equating:

(x_P - x0)^2 + (y_P - y0)^2 = (x_P + a)^2 + y_P^2.

Expand: x_P^2 - 2x_P x0 + x0^2 + y_P^2 - 2y_P y0 + y0^2 = x_P^2 + 2a x_P + a^2 + y_P^2.

Cancel x_P^2 and y_P^2: -2x_P x0 + x0^2 - 2y_P y0 + y0^2 = 2a x_P + a^2.

But x0^2+y0^2 = a^2, so substitute: -2x_P x0 - 2y_P y0 + a^2 = 2a x_P + a^2 => -2x_P x0 - 2y_P y0 = 2a x_P => divide 2: -x_P x0 - y_P y0 = a x_P => y_P y0 = -x_P x0 - a x_P = -x_P (x0+a). Thus y_P = -x_P (x0+a)/y0.

So we have y_P.

Now define v = P - A = (x_P - x0, y_P - y0). We'll need |v|^2 = (x_P - x0)^2 + (y_P - y0)^2.

Now compute A·v = x0(x_P-x0) + y0(y_P-y0) = x0 x_P - x0^2 + y0 y_P - y0^2 = (x0 x_P + y0 y_P) - (x0^2+y0^2) = (x0 x_P + y0 y_P) - a^2.

But we can compute x0 x_P + y0 y_P. Since y_P = -x_P (x0+a)/y0, then y0 y_P = -x_P (x0+a). So x0 x_P + y0 y_P = x0 x_P - x_P (x0+a) = -a x_P. Thus A·v = -a x_P - a^2 = -a(x_P + a). But x_P = (d+b-a)/2, so x_P + a = (d+b-a+2a)/2 = (d+b+a)/2. Thus A·v = -a (d+b+a)/2.

But note that we also had earlier A·v = -a(d+a+b)/2. Same.

Now compute (A-N)·v = (x0-d)(x_P-x0) + y0(y_P-y0) = (x0-d)(x_P-x0) + (x0 x_P + y0 y_P) - (x0^2+y0^2) = (x0-d)(x_P-x0) + (x0 x_P + y0 y_P) - a^2. But we know x0 x_P + y0 y_P = -a x_P. So = (x0-d)(x_P-x0) - a x_P - a^2 = (x0-d)(x_P-x0) - a(x_P + a). Expand (x0-d)(x_P-x0) = (x0-d)x_P - (x0-d)x0 = x0 x_P - d x_P - x0^2 + d x0 = (x0 x_P - x0^2) + d(x0 - x_P). Then total = (x0 x_P - x0^2) + d(x0 - x_P) - a x_P - a^2 = (x0 x_P - x0^2 - a^2) + d(x0 - x_P) - a x_P. But x0^2 + a^2? Actually x0^2+y0^2=a^2, so x0^2 = a^2 - y0^2, so x0^2 + a^2 = 2a^2 - y0^2? Not helpful. Instead, we can use the alternative expression we derived earlier: (A-N)·v = -b(d+a+b)/2. That is simpler.

We'll trust that result from earlier algebra: (A-N)·v = -b(d+a+b)/2. We'll re-derive quickly: Using the same method as for A·v but with N. Since N=(d,0), we have (A-N)·v = (x0-d)(x_P-x0) + y0(y_P-y0). But we can also compute using the property that A, N, v are related. Alternatively, we can use the fact that A lies on Γ, so (A-N)^2 = b^2. And we have the expression for A·v. We can also compute using the dot product with (A-N) and (P-A). Possibly we can derive (A-N)·(P-A) = -b(d+a+b)/2. This is what we got earlier. So we accept that.

Now define t_E = -2 (A-M)·v / |v|^2 = -2 (A·v)/|v|^2 = -2 * ( -a(d+a+b)/2 ) / |v|^2 = a(d+a+b)/|v|^2. Similarly, t_F = -2 (A-N)·v / |v|^2 = -2 * ( -b(d+a+b)/2 ) / |v|^2 = b(d+a+b)/|v|^2.

Thus E = A + t_E v, F = A + t_F v.

Now we need the circumcircle of triangle BEF. B = (x0, -y0). Note that B is the reflection of A across the x-axis (since M and N are on x-axis). So B = (x0, -y0). Also, note that E and F lie on line AP, which is not symmetric.

We can compute the circumcenter O1 of BEF. Since we have coordinates, we can compute using perpendicular bisectors. However we can find a more elegant approach: Show that the line through H parallel to AP is the radical axis of some circles or that it is the polar of H with respect to circle (BEF). But we can also directly compute the distance from O1 to L and compare to radius.

Let's try to compute O1 exactly.

Let’s denote:

A = (x0, y0), B = (x0, -y0). v = (dx, dy) = (x_P - x0, y_P - y0). And we have:

dx = x_P - x0 = (d+b-a)/2 - x0.
dy = y_P - y0 = -x_P(x0+a)/y0 - y0 = -(x_P(x0+a) + y0^2)/y0.

We also have the relationship from earlier: x0 dx + y0 dy = -a(d+a+b)/2. And (x0-d)dx + y0 dy = -b(d+a+b)/2.

We can also compute |v|^2 = dx^2+dy^2.

Now E = A + t_E v = (x0 + t_E dx, y0 + t_E dy).
F = A + t_F v = (x0 + t_F dx, y0 + t_F dy).

We need O1, the circumcenter of B, E, F.

Observation: Points E and F are collinear with A and P. So they lie on a line L_AP: A + s v. B is symmetric to A across x-axis. So triangle BEF: B is off the line, E and F are on the line.

We can compute O1 as the intersection of perpendicular bisector of EF (which is perpendicular to v) and perpendicular bisector of BE (or BF). Since we have coordinates, we can compute O1 using formulas.

Let’s set up variables.

Let’s define:

Let u = v, but we may also denote:

Let’s compute O1 by solving the system: O1 is equidistant to B, E, F. So |O1-B|^2 = |O1-E|^2 = |O1-F|^2.

We can find O1 as the point that lies on the perpendicular bisector of EF and also on the perpendicular bisector of BE.

We have EF is a segment of line AP. Its midpoint M_EF = ( (x_E+x_F)/2, (y_E+y_F)/2 ) = ( x0 + (t_E+t_F)dx/2, y0 + (t_E+t_F)dy/2 ). Since t_E = aK, t_F = bK, where K = (d+a+b)/|v|^2. So t_E+t_F = (a+b)K. And dx, dy are known.

The slope of EF is dy/dx (if dx≠0). So the perpendicular bisector of EF has direction vector perpendicular to v: e.g., n = (dy, -dx) (since dot (dx,dy)·(dy,-dx)=dx dy - dy dx=0). Actually (dy, -dx) is perpendicular to v. So line L_EF_perp: through M_EF with direction n.

Now perpendicular bisector of BE: B=(x0,-y0), E=(x0+t_E dx, y0+t_E dy). Midpoint M_BE = ( x0 + (t_E/2) dx, (-y0 + y0 + t_E dy)/2 = (x0 + (t_E/2)dx, (t_E dy)/2 )). So M_BE = (x0 + (t_E/2)dx, (t_E dy)/2). The slope of BE: (y_E - y_B)/(x_E - x_B) = (y0+t_E dy + y0)/(x0+t_E dx - x0) = (2y0 + t_E dy)/(t_E dx). So slope = (2y0 + t_E dy)/(t_E dx). Perpendicular slope = - (t_E dx)/(2y0 + t_E dy). So line L_BE_perp: through M_BE with that slope.

Finding intersection O1 may be messy. However we can use a known fact: In a triangle, the circumcenter can be expressed as the intersection of the perpendicular bisectors. Perhaps we can find O1 directly using vector formulas: O1 = ( (|B|^2 (E-F) + |E|^2 (F-B) + |F|^2 (B-E) ) / (2 (B×(E-F) + ... ) )? That's for complex numbers. Might be heavy.

Alternatively, we can use the fact that the circumcircle of BEF might be related to the circle with diameter something. Let's compute O1 in terms of A and v and maybe use symmetry.

Since B is the reflection of A across the x-axis, and E,F are on AP, perhaps we can find the circle (BEF) by considering the transformation: Reflect the line AP across x-axis to get a line through B and the reflection of P (call P' = (x_P, -y_P)). Then the points E and F reflect to points on that line, but not necessarily E' and F'. However, note that the reflection of the circle (BEF) across x-axis is the circle through A, E', F'? Actually reflection of B across x-axis is A. So if we reflect the whole configuration across x-axis, B goes to A, E goes to some point E' (reflection of E), F goes to F'. So the reflected circle is the circle through A, E', F'. But E' is the reflection of E across x-axis: since E lies on line AP, its reflection E' lies on the reflection of line AP, which is the line through B and the reflection of P? Wait, reflect line AP across x-axis: A reflects to B, P reflects to P' (since x_P same, y_P negative -> -y_P positive). So the reflection of line AP is line B P'. So E' and F' lie on line B P'. So the reflected circle is the circle through A, E', F'. But A, E', F' are not necessarily collinear? Actually E' and F' lie on line B P', so A is not on that line generally. So the reflected circle is some circle through A and two points on line B P'. That is similar to the original but with roles swapped. Not simpler.

Another approach: Use analytic geometry but with complex numbers. Set up coordinates as before. Then compute the circumcenter O1 of BEF using algebraic formulas. Since we have expressions for t_E and t_F, we might be able to simplify O1. Let's attempt to compute O1 using the condition that O1 is the point that satisfies:

(O1 - B)^2 = (O1 - E)^2 and (O1 - B)^2 = (O1 - F)^2.

We can subtract to get linear equations. Let's define O1 = (x, y). Then:

|O1-B|^2 = (x-x0)^2 + (y+y0)^2.
|O1-E|^2 = (x - (x0+t_E dx))^2 + (y - (y0+t_E dy))^2.
Set equal:

(x-x0)^2 + (y+y0)^2 = (x-x0 - t_E dx)^2 + (y-y0 - t_E dy)^2.

Expand both sides. LHS: (x-x0)^2 + (y+y0)^2 = (x-x0)^2 + y^2 + 2y y0 + y0^2.
RHS: (x-x0)^2 - 2t_E dx (x-x0) + t_E^2 dx^2 + (y-y0)^2 - 2t_E dy (y-y0) + t_E^2 dy^2 = (x-x0)^2 + (y-y0)^2 - 2t_E (dx (x-x0) + dy (y-y0)) + t_E^2 (dx^2+dy^2).

Now note that (y-y0)^2 = y^2 - 2y y0 + y0^2. So RHS = (x-x0)^2 + y^2 - 2y y0 + y0^2 - 2t_E (dx (x-x0) + dy (y-y0)) + t_E^2 |v|^2.

Thus equality becomes:

(x-x0)^2 + y^2 + 2y y0 + y0^2 = (x-x0)^2 + y^2 - 2y y0 + y0^2 - 2t_E (dx (x-x0) + dy (y-y0)) + t_E^2 |v|^2.

Cancel (x-x0)^2 and y^2 and y0^2:

2y y0 = -2y y0 - 2t_E (dx (x-x0) + dy (y-y0)) + t_E^2 |v|^2.

So 4y y0 = -2t_E (dx (x-x0) + dy (y-y0)) + t_E^2 |v|^2.

Thus 2y y0 = - (t_E/2) (dx (x-x0) + dy (y-y0)) + (t_E^2 |v|^2)/4. (Equation A)

Similarly, from |O1-B|^2 = |O1-F|^2 we get:

4y y0 = -2t_F (dx (x-x0) + dy (y-y0)) + t_F^2 |v|^2. (Equation B)

Now we have two equations involving the linear form L(x,y) = dx (x-x0) + dy (y-y0). This is the dot product of (x-x0, y-y0) with v. Actually note that v = (dx, dy). So L = (x-x0, y-y0)·v.

We can solve for L and y.

From Equation A: 2y y0 = - (t_E/2) L + (t_E^2 |v|^2)/4.
From Equation B: 2y y0 = - (t_F/2) L + (t_F^2 |v|^2)/4.

Thus equate:

- (t_E/2) L + (t_E^2 |v|^2)/4 = - (t_F/2) L + (t_F^2 |v|^2)/4.

Multiply by 4: -2 t_E L + t_E^2 |v|^2 = -2 t_F L + t_F^2 |v|^2 => bring terms: -2 t_E L + 2 t_F L = t_F^2 |v|^2 - t_E^2 |v|^2 => 2L (t_F - t_E) = |v|^2 (t_F^2 - t_E^2) = |v|^2 (t_F - t_E)(t_F + t_E). If t_F ≠ t_E (since a≠b, t_E≠t_F), we can divide by (t_F - t_E): 2L = |v|^2 (t_F + t_E). Thus L = |v|^2 (t_E + t_F)/2.

Now t_E = aK, t_F = bK, where K = (d+a+b)/|v|^2. So t_E+t_F = (a+b)K = (a+b)(d+a+b)/|v|^2. Then L = |v|^2 * ( (a+b)(d+a+b)/|v|^2 ) / 2 = (a+b)(d+a+b)/2.

Thus we have L = (a+b)(d+a+b)/2.

Now we can also compute y from one equation: 2y y0 = - (t_E/2) L + (t_E^2 |v|^2)/4 = - (t_E/2)L + (t_E^2 |v|^2)/4. But we can also express y directly. However note that we want O1 = (x,y). We have L = dx (x-x0) + dy (y-y0) = (x-x0, y-y0)·v. This gives a linear relation. Also we need O1 to be equidistant to B and E, but we already used that to get L. We still need to determine x and y individually. But perhaps we don't need O1 explicitly; we just need the distance from O1 to line L. However we can find O1 by solving L = constant and also the fact that O1 lies on the perpendicular bisector of EF? Actually we used the equality of distances to B and E to derive L; that's one linear condition. Similarly, equality to B and F gives another linear condition that we already used to get L? Actually we used both equalities to derive L and then we still have the condition from one of them to relate y. But we still need to determine O1 completely. However note that the perpendicular bisector of EF is given by a linear equation? Actually the perpendicular bisector of EF is the set of points equidistant to E and F. That is a linear equation because |O1-E|^2 = |O1-F|^2 yields: (x - x_E)^2+(y-y_E)^2 = (x-x_F)^2+(y-y_F)^2 => -2x(x_E-x_F)-2y(y_E-y_F)+x_E^2+y_E^2 = x_F^2+y_F^2? Actually simpler: (x_E-x_F)x + (y_E-y_F)y = (x_E^2+y_E^2 - x_F^2 - y_F^2)/2. So it's a line. So O1 is the intersection of two lines: the perpendicular bisector of EF and the line we derived from equating distances to B and E (or B and F). But we used both B-E and B-F to derive L, which is actually the condition that O1 is equidistant to B and E? Wait, we used |O1-B|^2 = |O1-E|^2 to get an equation involving L and y. That equation is not linear because it involves y and L; but we also have L = something, which is linear? Actually L = dx(x-x0)+dy(y-y0) is linear in x,y. And the equation from |O1-B|^2 = |O1-E|^2 gave us: 2y y0 = - (t_E/2) L + (t_E^2 |v|^2)/4. This is linear in y and L, but since L is linear in x,y, the whole equation is linear in x,y. Similarly, the other gives another linear equation. So O1 is determined by two linear equations. So we can solve for x,y.

Let's do that. We have L = dx (x-x0) + dy (y-y0). And we also have from the condition |O1-B|^2 = |O1-E|^2:

2y y0 = - (t_E/2) L + (t_E^2 |v|^2)/4.   (Eq1)

Similarly, from |O1-B|^2 = |O1-F|^2 we get:

2y y0 = - (t_F/2) L + (t_F^2 |v|^2)/4.   (Eq2)

But we already used these to find L; they are consistent. So we can use Eq1 (or Eq2) to get a linear relation between x and y.

Now, we also have the condition that O1 is equidistant to E and F? Actually we haven't used that. But if O1 satisfies |O1-B|^2 = |O1-E|^2 and |O1-B|^2 = |O1-F|^2, then automatically |O1-E|^2 = |O1-F|^2, so O1 lies on the perpendicular bisector of EF. So we don't need an extra condition; we can use Eq1 and the fact that L = constant (which we derived from Eq1 and Eq2) to solve. But note: L = constant came from equating Eq1 and Eq2, so it's not an independent condition. So we have two linear equations: Eq1 and L = constant. That should determine O1 uniquely.

Thus we have:

L = dx (x-x0) + dy (y-y0) = (a+b)(d+a+b)/2.   (1)

And

2y y0 = - (t_E/2) L + (t_E^2 |v|^2)/4.   (2)

Now we need to compute y and x. But maybe we can find O1 more directly by using vector geometry: Since B is symmetric to A, and E,F are on line through A with direction v, perhaps O1 is the point that is the reflection of something? Let's try to compute O1 explicitly in terms of a,b,d,y0,x0.

We have already computed L = (a+b)(d+a+b)/2. That's a nice constant.

Now we also have from Eq1: 2y y0 = - (t_E/2) L + (t_E^2 |v|^2)/4 = (t_E/2)( -L + (t_E |v|^2)/2 ).

But t_E |v|^2 = a(d+a+b). So (t_E |v|^2)/2 = a(d+a+b)/2. And L = (a+b)(d+a+b)/2. So -L + (t_E |v|^2)/2 = -(a+b)(d+a+b)/2 + a(d+a+b)/2 = (d+a+b)/2 * ( -a-b + a) = (d+a+b)/2 * (-b) = -b(d+a+b)/2. Thus Eq1 becomes:

Thus y = - (a b (d+a+b)^2)/(8 y0 |v|^2).   (3)

Now we also have L = dx (x-x0) + dy (y-y0) = (a+b)(d+a+b)/2.

Given the complexity, we can compute O1 explicitly in terms of a,b,d and y0. Let's do that.

We have:

We also have:

dy = y_P - y0.

We need to compute |v|^2 = dx^2+dy^2. We can also compute dx and dy in terms of x0,y0,d,a,b. Since we have x0 = (d^2+a^2-b^2)/(2d). And y0 = sqrt(a^2 - x0^2). But we can keep y0 as symbolic, using that x0^2+y0^2=a^2 and (d-x0)^2+y0^2=b^2. So we can derive relationships.

Let’s compute dx and dy more explicitly.

We have x_P = (d+b-a)/2. Then

dx = x_P - x0 = (d+b-a)/2 - x0.

We can express x0 = (d^2+a^2-b^2)/(2d). So

dx = (d+b-a)/2 - (d^2+a^2-b^2)/(2d) = [ d(d+b-a) - (d^2+a^2-b^2) ] / (2d) = [ d^2+bd-ad - d^2 - a^2 + b^2 ] / (2d) = [ bd - ad - a^2 + b^2 ] / (2d) = [ d(b-a) - (a^2 - b^2) ] / (2d) = [ d(b-a) + (b^2 - a^2) ] / (2d) = [ d(b-a) + (b-a)(b+a) ] / (2d) = (b-a)(d+a+b)/(2d).

So dx = (b-a)(d+a+b)/(2d). Good.

Now dy = y_P - y0. We have y_P = -x_P (x0+a)/y0. So

dy = -x_P (x0+a)/y0 - y0 = -(x_P (x0+a) + y0^2)/y0.

Now x_P = (d+b-a)/2. And x0+a =? We can compute x0+a = (d^2+a^2-b^2)/(2d) + a = (d^2+a^2-b^2+2ad)/(2d) = (d^2+2ad+a^2-b^2)/(2d) = ((d+a)^2 - b^2)/(2d) = ((d+a-b)(d+a+b))/(2d). So x0+a = ((d+a-b)(d+a+b))/(2d). Then

x_P (x0+a) = ((d+b-a)/2) * ((d+a-b)(d+a+b))/(2d) = ( (d+b-a)(d+a-b)(d+a+b) ) / (4d).

Now y0^2 = a^2 - x0^2 = a^2 - ((d^2+a^2-b^2)/(2d))^2 = (4a^2d^2 - (d^2+a^2-b^2)^2)/(4d^2). That is messy. But we might also compute x_P (x0+a) + y0^2 in a nicer way. But maybe we can find a simpler expression: Since C and D are on x-axis, and A is known, we can compute the circumradius R_ACD = (|AC| |AD| |CD|)/(4Δ). But we can compute Δ = area of triangle ACD. Also side lengths: AC = distance between A and C = sqrt((x0+a)^2+y0^2). But note that (x0+a)^2+y0^2 =? Since A is on Ω: x0^2+y0^2=a^2, so (x0+a)^2+y0^2 = x0^2+2a x0+a^2+y0^2 = (x0^2+y0^2)+a^2+2a x0 = 2a^2+2a x0 = 2a(a+x0). So AC = sqrt(2a(a+x0)). Similarly, AD = distance between A and D = sqrt((x0-(d+b))^2+y0^2). Now CD = (d+b+a) as said.

So the circumradius R_ACD = (AC * AD * CD) / (4Δ) = ( sqrt(2a(a+x0)) * sqrt(2b(b+d-x0)) * (d+b+a) ) / (4 * (1/2)*(d+b+a)*y0 ) = (2 sqrt(ab(a+x0)(b+d-x0)) * (d+b+a) ) / (2 (d+b+a) y0 ) = ( sqrt(ab(a+x0)(b+d-x0)) ) / y0.

Given the analytic approach is manageable, let's proceed with it. We'll compute O1 = (x,y) explicitly in terms of a,b,d and y0. Then compute distance from O1 to line L. We already derived:

y = - (a b (d+a+b)^2)/(8 y0 |v|^2).   (3)

But |v|^2 = ab (a+x0)(b+d-x0)/y0^2. So substitute:

|v|^2 = ab (a+x0)(b+d-x0)/y0^2.

Then y = - (a b (d+a+b)^2)/(8 y0 * (ab (a+x0)(b+d-x0)/y0^2) ) = - (a b (d+a+b)^2 * y0^2)/(8 y0 * ab (a+x0)(b+d-x0)) = - ( (d+a+b)^2 * y0 )/(8 (a+x0)(b+d-x0) ).

So y = - ( (d+a+b)^2 y0 )/(8 (a+x0)(b+d-x0) ).   (3')

Now we also need x. From L = dx (x-x0) + dy (y-y0) = (a+b)(d+a+b)/2.

We have dx = (b-a)(d+a+b)/(2d). Also we have dy = y_P - y0 = -x_P (x0+a)/y0 - y0 = -(x_P(x0+a)+y0^2)/y0. But we can also compute dy using the expression from earlier: dy = (d+a+b)/(2 y0) * ( -a - x0(b-a)/d ). But perhaps we can compute dy in terms of known quantities like (a+x0)(b+d-x0). Let's derive dy from the formula for |v|^2.

We have v = (dx, dy). And we know |v|^2 = dx^2+dy^2 = ab (a+x0)(b+d-x0)/y0^2. Also we have dx known. So we can find dy^2 = |v|^2 - dx^2. But we need dy itself, not just squared, because the linear equation involves dy. However we might be able to compute dy directly using the dot product condition we already used: x0 dx + y0 dy = -a(d+a+b)/2. So we can solve for dy:

dy = ( -a(d+a+b)/2 - x0 dx ) / y0.

We have x0 dx = x0 * (b-a)(d+a+b)/(2d) = (b-a)(d+a+b) x0/(2d). So

dy = ( -a(d+a+b)/2 - (b-a)(d+a+b) x0/(2d) ) / y0 = -(d+a+b)/(2y0) * ( a + (b-a)x0/d ).

Thus dy = - (d+a+b)/(2y0) * ( a + (b-a)x0/d ). That is the expression.

Now we also have y0 dy from earlier: y0 dy = - (d+a+b)/2 * ( a + (b-a)x0/d ). So dy = - (d+a+b)/(2y0) * ( a + (b-a)x0/d ).

Now we can compute dy (y - y0). We have y from (3'). So we need to compute dy (y - y0). This might simplify.

First, note that a + (b-a)x0/d =? We can also express x0 in terms of a,b,d. x0 = (d^2+a^2-b^2)/(2d). So compute (b-a)x0/d = (b-a)*(d^2+a^2-b^2)/(2d^2). Then a + that = a + (b-a)(d^2+a^2-b^2)/(2d^2) = [2a d^2 + (b-a)(d^2+a^2-b^2)]/(2d^2). Expand (b-a)(d^2+a^2-b^2) = (b-a)d^2 + (b-a)(a^2-b^2) = (b-a)d^2 + (b-a)(a-b)(a+b) = (b-a)d^2 - (b-a)^2 (a+b). So numerator = 2a d^2 + (b-a)d^2 - (b-a)^2 (a+b) = d^2(2a + b - a) - (b-a)^2 (a+b) = d^2(a+b) - (b-a)^2 (a+b) = (a+b)(d^2 - (b-a)^2) = (a+b)(d^2 - (b-a)^2). So a + (b-a)x0/d = (a+b)(d^2 - (b-a)^2)/(2d^2). But d^2 - (b-a)^2 = (d - (b-a))(d + (b-a)) = (d + a - b)(d + b - a). So a + (b-a)x0/d = (a+b)(d+a-b)(d+b-a)/(2d^2). Good.

Thus dy = - (d+a+b)/(2y0) * [ (a+b)(d+a-b)(d+b-a)/(2d^2) ] = - (d+a+b)(a+b)(d+a-b)(d+b-a)/(4d^2 y0).

Now we also have (a+x0)(b+d-x0) =? We computed a+x0 = ((d+a-b)(d+a+b))/(2d), and b+d-x0 = ((d+b-a)(d+b+a))/(2d). So product = ((d+a-b)(d+b-a)(d+a+b)^2)/(4d^2). So (a+x0)(b+d-x0) = ((d+a-b)(d+b-a)(d+a+b)^2)/(4d^2). Then dy = - (d+a+b)(a+b)(d+a-b)(d+b-a)/(4d^2 y0) = - (a+b)(d+a-b)(d+b-a)(d+a+b)/(4d^2 y0). Compare to (a+x0)(b+d-x0) which has factor (d+a+b)^2, not (d+a+b). So dy = - (a+b)/(d+a+b) * (a+x0)(b+d-x0)/y0? Actually (a+x0)(b+d-x0) = ((d+a-b)(d+b-a)(d+a+b)^2)/(4d^2). So (d+a-b)(d+b-a)/(4d^2) = (a+x0)(b+d-x0)/( (d+a+b)^2 y0^2? Not. Let's keep dy as is.

Now we need to compute dy (y - y0). We have y = - ( (d+a+b)^2 y0 )/(8 (a+x0)(b+d-x0) ). So y - y0 = -y0[ (d+a+b)^2/(8 (a+x0)(b+d-x0) + 1 ] = -y0[ ((d+a+b)^2 + 8 (a+x0)(b+d-x0))/(8 (a+x0)(b+d-x0)) ].

Thus dy (y - y0) = dy * (-y0) * ( (d+a+b)^2 + 8 (a+x0)(b+d-x0) )/(8 (a+x0)(b+d-x0) ). But dy = - (a+b)(d+a-b)(d+b-a)(d+a+b)/(4d^2 y0). So dy * (-y0) = (a+b)(d+a-b)(d+b-a)(d+a+b)/(4d^2). Then dy(y-y0) = (a+b)(d+a-b)(d+b-a)(d+a+b)/(4d^2) * ( (d+a+b)^2 + 8 (a+x0)(b+d-x0) )/(8 (a+x0)(b+d-x0)) = (a+b)(d+a-b)(d+b-a)(d+a+b) ( (d+a+b)^2 + 8 (a+x0)(b+d-x0) )/(32 d^2 (a+x0)(b+d-x0)).

Now we also have L = dx (x-x0) + dy (y-y0). And we know L = (a+b)(d+a+b)/2. So dx (x-x0) = L - dy(y-y0) = (a+b)(d+a+b)/2 - dy(y-y0). So x = x0 + (L - dy(y-y0))/dx.

We could compute x explicitly, but maybe we can compute the distance from O1 to line L directly using the formula for the distance from a point to a line. The line L: through H parallel to AP. We have H = (x_P, y_H) with y_H = x_P(d-x_P)/y_P. And direction v = (dx, dy). So the line L equation can be written as: (X - H) is perpendicular to n = (dy, -dx) (since n·v=0). Actually n = (dy, -dx) is perpendicular to v because dot(dx,dy)·(dy,-dx)=dx dy - dy dx =0. So line L: n·(X - H)=0 => dy (x - x_P) - dx (y - y_H)=0 => dy x - dx y = dy x_P - dx y_H. So line L: dy x - dx y = dy x_P - dx y_H.

Now we want to compute the distance from O1 = (x,y) to this line. The distance d = |dy x - dx y - (dy x_P - dx y_H)| / sqrt(dy^2 + dx^2) = |dy(x - x_P) - dx(y - y_H)| / |v|.

But note that the numerator is exactly the absolute value of the cross product of (x-x_P, y-y_H) with v. That is the distance times |v|. So d = |(x-x_P, y-y_H) × v| / |v|. So d = |(x-x_P)dy - (y-y_H)dx| / |v|.

But we might be able to compute this using known relationships. Since we have expressions for x and y, we could compute (x-x_P)dy - (y-y_H)dx. However maybe we can find that this numerator equals something like |v| * R1, giving d = R1.

Given the algebraic complexity, we can try to compute directly using symbolic algebra but we need to present a clean proof. Perhaps we can find a more synthetic way.

Let's attempt synthetic reasoning again with insights from the analytic derivations.

We have:

- P is circumcenter of ACD.
- AP meets Ω at E and Γ at F. So E and F are the second intersections of line AP with the circles.
- B is the other intersection of Ω and Γ.
- H is orthocenter of triangle PMN.
- We want to show that line through H parallel to AP is tangent to circle (BEF).

Observations:

1. Since M and N are centers, AB is the common chord, and MN is the line of centers. So O = midpoint of AB lies on MN, and MO ⟂ AB, NO ⟂ AB. So O is the foot of perpendiculars from M,N to AB.

2. Points C and D are the endpoints of the diameters of Ω and Γ on MN? Actually C is the leftmost point of Ω, D is the rightmost point of Γ. So MC = a, ND = b. Also, note that C, M, N, D are collinear in that order.

3. Triangle ACD: A is intersection, C and D on MN. Its circumcenter P. Since C and D are on MN, the perpendicular bisector of CD is perpendicular to MN, and P lies on it. So AP is a line from A to that perpendicular bisector. Not necessarily perpendicular to CD.

4. We derived that t_E = a(d+a+b)/AP^2 and t_F = b(d+a+b)/AP^2. So AE = a(d+a+b)/AP, AF = b(d+a+b)/AP. Also note that AC = sqrt(2a(a+x0)) =? Might relate to something like the power of A with respect to circle with diameter something.

5. Also we found that L = (a+b)(d+a+b)/2, which is the dot product of (x-x0, y-y0) with v. This constant came from equating distances to B and E. So that is a key relation: The circumcenter O1 of BEF satisfies (O1-A)·v = L. In other words, the projection of O1 onto line AP (with direction v) is at a certain distance from A.

6. We also found y = - ((d+a+b)^2 y0)/(8 (a+x0)(b+d-x0)). And we can also compute x. Maybe we can find that O1 is the point that divides the segment connecting the midpoint of AB and something? Let's compute O1 numerically for our example: O1 = (3/4, -9√15/20). That is (0.75, -1.74284). The midpoint of AB is O = (x0,0) = (-0.25,0). So O1 is not obviously related.

7. But note that H = (5/4, -5/(4√15)) = (1.25, -0.3228). And the line through H parallel to AP: we derived its equation √15 x + 3y = √15. And we found that distance from O1 to this line is R1. So the tangent condition holds.

Now, to prove it generally, we can use the coordinate setup and compute the distance. Since we have expressions for all points in terms of a,b,d,y0, we can compute O1 as (x,y) and then compute the distance to line L. This is a computational proof. We need to ensure that the algebra is correct and that we don't make mistakes. We can simplify by using vector methods and known relations.

Let's attempt to compute O1 explicitly. We have:

L = (a+b)(d+a+b)/2.

We also have from Eq1: 2y y0 = - (t_E/2) L + (t_E^2 |v|^2)/4. But we already used that to get y. So y is given.

Now we need x. Use L = dx (x-x0) + dy (y-y0). So x = x0 + (L - dy(y-y0))/dx.

Now compute dx and dy in terms of a,b,d,y0. We have dx = (b-a)(d+a+b)/(2d). And dy = - (a+b)(d+a-b)(d+b-a)(d+a+b)/(4d^2 y0). Also we have y from (3'): y = - ( (d+a+b)^2 y0 )/(8 (a+x0)(b+d-x0) ). And we have expressions for (a+x0)(b+d-x0). Also note that a+x0 = ((d+a-b)(d+a+b))/(2d). And b+d-x0 = ((d+b-a)(d+b+a))/(2d). So (a+x0)(b+d-x0) = ((d+a-b)(d+b-a)(d+a+b)^2)/(4d^2). Then y = - ( (d+a+b)^2 y0 )/(8 * ((d+a-b)(d+b-a)(d+a+b)^2/(4d^2) ) = - ( (d+a+b)^2 y0 * 4d^2 )/(8 (d+a-b)(d+b-a)(d+a+b)^2) = - (d^2 y0)/(2 (d+a-b)(d+b-a) ). So y = - (d^2 y0)/(2 (d+a-b)(d+b-a) ).   (3'')

That's much simpler! Good. So we have:

y = - (d^2 y0) / (2 (d+a-b)(d+b-a) ).

Now also we have dy from earlier: dy = - (a+b)(d+a-b)(d+b-a)(d+a+b)/(4d^2 y0). So dy = - (a+b)(d+a-b)(d+b-a)(d+a+b)/(4d^2 y0). Then dy(y-y0) = dy * y - dy y0. But we need dy(y-y0) in the expression for x. However maybe we can compute (L - dy(y-y0))/dx directly.

First, compute L = (a+b)(d+a+b)/2.

Now compute dy(y-y0) = dy*y - dy y0. dy y = dy * ( - d^2 y0/(2 (d+a-b)(d+b-a)) ) = - d^2 y0 dy / (2 (d+a-b)(d+b-a)). And dy y0 = - (a+b)(d+a-b)(d+b-a)(d+a+b)/(4d^2 y0) * y0 = - (a+b)(d+a-b)(d+b-a)(d+a+b)/(4d^2). So dy(y-y0) = dy*y - dy y0 = - d^2 y0 dy/(2 (d+a-b)(d+b-a)) - ( - (a+b)(d+a-b)(d+b-a)(d+a+b)/(4d^2) )? Actually careful: dy(y-y0) = dy*y - dy*y0. dy*y = dy * ( - d^2 y0/(2 (d+a-b)(d+b-a)) ) = - (d^2 y0) dy/(2 (d+a-b)(d+b-a)). dy*y0 = dy * y0 = - (a+b)(d+a-b)(d+b-a)(d+a+b)/(4d^2) (since y0 cancels). So dy(y-y0) = - (d^2 y0) dy/(2 (d+a-b)(d+b-a)) + (a+b)(d+a-b)(d+b-a)(d+a+b)/(4d^2). But we have dy expression. So substitute dy:

dy = - (a+b)(d+a-b)(d+b-a)(d+a+b)/(4d^2 y0). Then

- (d^2 y0) dy/(2 (d+a-b)(d+b-a)) = - (d^2 y0)/(2 (d+a-b)(d+b-a)) * ( - (a+b)(d+a-b)(d+b-a)(d+a+b)/(4d^2 y0) ) = - (d^2 y0) * (- (a+b)(d+a-b)(d+b-a)(d+a+b))/(2 (d+a-b)(d+b-a) * 4d^2 y0) = (d^2 y0)*(a+b)(d+a-b)(d+b-a)(d+a+b)/(8 d^2 y0 (d+a-b)(d+b-a)) = (a+b)(d+a+b)/8. The factor (d+a-b)(d+b-a) cancels. So that term simplifies to (a+b)(d+a+b)/8.

Thus dy(y-y0) = (a+b)(d+a+b)/8 + (a+b)(d+a-b)(d+b-a)(d+a+b)/(4d^2). Wait, we also have the second part: + (a+b)(d+a-b)(d+b-a)(d+a+b)/(4d^2). So dy(y-y0) = (a+b)(d+a+b)/8 + (a+b)(d+a-b)(d+b-a)(d+a+b)/(4d^2). Factor (a+b)(d+a+b): = (a+b)(d+a+b)[ 1/8 + (d+a-b)(d+b-a)/(4d^2) ].

Now L = (a+b)(d+a+b)/2 = 4*(a+b)(d+a+b)/8. So L - dy(y-y0) = (a+b)(d+a+b)/2 - dy(y-y0) = (a+b)(d+a+b)[ 1/2 - 1/8 - (d+a-b)(d+b-a)/(4d^2) ] = (a+b)(d+a+b)[ 3/8 - (d+a-b)(d+b-a)/(4d^2) ].

Now we need to divide by dx. dx = (b-a)(d+a+b)/(2d). So

(L - dy(y-y0))/dx = (a+b)(d+a+b)[ 3/8 - (d+a-b)(d+b-a)/(4d^2) ] / ( (b-a)(d+a+b)/(2d) ) = (a+b)(d+a+b) * [ 3/8 - (d+a-b)(d+b-a)/(4d^2) ] * (2d)/( (b-a)(d+a+b) ) = (a+b) * (2d)/(b-a) * [ 3/8 - (d+a-b)(d+b-a)/(4d^2) ].

So x = x0 + (a+b) * (2d)/(b-a) * [ 3/8 - (d+a-b)(d+b-a)/(4d^2) ].

Now we need to compute x0. x0 = (d^2 + a^2 - b^2)/(2d). We can simplify the expression.

Let’s compute the term inside brackets: T = 3/8 - (d+a-b)(d+b-a)/(4d^2). Multiply by 2d/(b-a) to get something.

First, note that (d+a-b)(d+b-a) = (d + (a-b))(d - (a-b)) = d^2 - (a-b)^2 = d^2 - (a^2 -2ab+b^2) = d^2 - a^2 +2ab - b^2. So (d+a-b)(d+b-a) = d^2 - a^2 - b^2 + 2ab.

Thus T = 3/8 - (d^2 - a^2 - b^2 + 2ab)/(4d^2) = 3/8 - (1/4) + (a^2+b^2-2ab)/(4d^2) = 3/8 - 2/8 + (a^2+b^2-2ab)/(4d^2) = 1/8 + (a^2+b^2-2ab)/(4d^2) = 1/8 + (a-b)^2/(4d^2). So T = 1/8 + (a-b)^2/(4d^2). Good.

Then (a+b)*2d/(b-a) * T = (a+b)*2d/(b-a) * [1/8 + (a-b)^2/(4d^2)]. But note that (a-b)^2 = (b-a)^2. So (a+b)*2d/(b-a) * (a-b)^2/(4d^2) = (a+b)*2d/(b-a) * (b-a)^2/(4d^2) = (a+b)*2d*(b-a)/(4d^2) = (a+b)(b-a)/(2d). And the other part: (a+b)*2d/(b-a) * (1/8) = (a+b)*2d/(8(b-a)) = (a+b)d/(4(b-a)). So

x = x0 + (a+b)d/(4(b-a)) + (a+b)(b-a)/(2d).

Now compute x0 = (d^2 + a^2 - b^2)/(2d). So

x = (d^2 + a^2 - b^2)/(2d) + (a+b)d/(4(b-a)) + (a+b)(b-a)/(2d). Combine the terms with denominator 2d: (d^2 + a^2 - b^2)/(2d) + (a+b)(b-a)/(2d) = (d^2 + a^2 - b^2 + (a+b)(b-a))/(2d). Compute (a+b)(b-a) = (b^2 - a^2). So numerator: d^2 + a^2 - b^2 + b^2 - a^2 = d^2. So that sum = d^2/(2d) = d/2. So x = d/2 + (a+b)d/(4(b-a)). So

x = d/2 + (a+b)d/(4(b-a)).   (4)

Thus O1 = (x, y) where:

x = d/2 + (a+b)d/(4(b-a)).
y = - (d^2 y0)/(2 (d+a-b)(d+b-a)).

Now we have O1 coordinates. We also need H. H = (x_P, y_H) with x_P = (d+b-a)/2, y_H = x_P(d-x_P)/y_P. But we can also compute y_H in terms of a,b,d,y0. Since y_P = -x_P (x0+a)/y0. Then y_H = x_P(d-x_P)/y_P = x_P(d-x_P) * ( -y0/(x_P(x0+a)) ) = - y0 (d-x_P)/(x0+a). So y_H = - y0 (d - x_P)/(x0+a). Now x_P = (d+b-a)/2. Then d - x_P = d - (d+b-a)/2 = (2d - d - b + a)/2 = (d - b + a)/2 = (d + a - b)/2. And x0+a =? x0 = (d^2+a^2-b^2)/(2d). So x0+a = (d^2+a^2-b^2+2ad)/(2d) = ((d+a)^2 - b^2)/(2d) = (d+a-b)(d+a+b)/(2d). So y_H = - y0 * ( (d+a-b)/2 ) / ( (d+a-b)(d+a+b)/(2d) ) = - y0 * ( (d+a-b)/2 ) * (2d/((d+a-b)(d+a+b))) = - y0 * d/(d+a+b). So y_H = - (d y0)/(d+a+b). Good.

Thus H = (x_P, y_H) = ( (d+b-a)/2, - d y0/(d+a+b) ). 

Now we need line L through H parallel to AP. AP direction v = (dx, dy). But we might also derive the line equation from the condition that it is parallel to AP. However we can find the line equation using the fact that its direction is v, so it is perpendicular to n = (dy, -dx). So line L: n·(X - H)=0 => dy (x - x_P) - dx (y - y_H)=0 => dy x - dx y = dy x_P - dx y_H.

We already have dy and dx. But we can also express line L in terms of known constants. Let's compute dy and dx in terms of a,b,d,y0. We have:

dx = (b-a)(d+a+b)/(2d).
dy = - (a+b)(d+a-b)(d+b-a)(d+a+b)/(4d^2 y0).

We can also compute dy x_P - dx y_H. But perhaps there is a simpler way to get the line equation: Since we have H and we know the direction is v, we can write the line as: (x,y) = H + t v. So parametric. Then we want to compute distance from O1 to this line. We can compute using the cross product formula: d = |(O1 - H) × v| / |v|. So d = |(x - x_P) dy - (y - y_H) dx| / |v|.

Now we have expressions for x, y, x_P, y_H, dx, dy, |v|. Let's compute numerator N = |(x - x_P) dy - (y - y_H) dx|. We can try to simplify.

First, compute x - x_P. From (4): x = d/2 + (a+b)d/(4(b-a)). And x_P = (d+b-a)/2. So

x - x_P = d/2 + (a+b)d/(4(b-a)) - (d+b-a)/2 = d/2 - (d+b-a)/2 + (a+b)d/(4(b-a)) = (d - (d+b-a))/2 + (a+b)d/(4(b-a)) = (d - d - b + a)/2 + (a+b)d/(4(b-a)) = (a - b)/2 + (a+b)d/(4(b-a)). Note that (a-b)/2 = -(b-a)/2. So

x - x_P = -(b-a)/2 + (a+b)d/(4(b-a)). Factor (b-a): = (b-a)[ -1/2 + (a+b)d/(4(b-a)^2) ]? Actually (a+b)d/(4(b-a)) = (a+b)d/(4(b-a)). So it's not directly factorable with (b-a) in denominator. But we can write:

x - x_P = (a+b)d/(4(b-a)) - (b-a)/2. Put over common denominator 4(b-a): = [ (a+b)d - 2(b-a)^2 ] / (4(b-a)). So

x - x_P = [ (a+b)d - 2(b-a)^2 ] / (4(b-a)).   (5)

Now compute y - y_H. y = - d^2 y0/(2 (d+a-b)(d+b-a)). And y_H = - d y0/(d+a+b). So y - y_H = - d^2 y0/(2 (d+a-b)(d+b-a)) + d y0/(d+a+b) = d y0 [ - d/(2 (d+a-b)(d+b-a)) + 1/(d+a+b) ] = d y0 [ (1/(d+a+b)) - d/(2 (d+a-b)(d+b-a)) ].

Now compute N = (x - x_P) dy - (y - y_H) dx. We have dy and dx expressions. Let's substitute:

dy = - (a+b)(d+a-b)(d+b-a)(d+a+b)/(4d^2 y0).
dx = (b-a)(d+a+b)/(2d).

So

(x - x_P) dy = [ (a+b)d - 2(b-a)^2 ] / (4(b-a)) * [ - (a+b)(d+a-b)(d+b-a)(d+a+b)/(4d^2 y0) ] = - [ (a+b)d - 2(b-a)^2 ] (a+b)(d+a-b)(d+b-a)(d+a+b) / (16 d^2 y0 (b-a)).

Similarly, (y - y_H) dx = d y0 [ (1/(d+a+b)) - d/(2 (d+a-b)(d+b-a)) ] * (b-a)(d+a+b)/(2d) = y0 [ (b-a)(d+a+b)/(2d) * d * (1/(d+a+b) - d/(2 (d+a-b)(d+b-a)) )? Actually careful: (y - y_H) dx = d y0 * [ ... ] * (b-a)(d+a+b)/(2d) = y0 * [ ... ] * (b-a)(d+a+b)/2. So

(y - y_H) dx = y0 * (b-a)(d+a+b)/2 * [ 1/(d+a+b) - d/(2 (d+a-b)(d+b-a)) ] = y0 * (b-a)/2 * [ 1 - d(d+a+b)/(2 (d+a-b)(d+b-a)) ].

But perhaps we can find a more clever synthetic proof. Let's think about the geometry.

We have circles Ω and Γ intersecting at A and B. Line MN meets Ω at C and Γ at D. P is circumcenter of ACD. AP meets Ω again at E and Γ again at F. H is orthocenter of triangle PMN.

Goal: line through H parallel to AP is tangent to circle (BEF).

Observations:

- Since M and N are centers, the radical axis of Ω and Γ is AB. So for any point X on AB, the power with respect to Ω and Γ are equal. In particular, for point O (midpoint of AB), we have OM ⟂ AB, ON ⟂ AB, and OM^2 =? Not.

- Points C and D are the points where MN meets the circles. So MC = a, ND = b. Also, note that the power of M with respect to Γ is? Not needed.

- Triangle ACD: A is on both circles, C on Ω, D on Γ. Its circumcenter P. Since C and D are on MN, the perpendicular bisector of CD is perpendicular to MN, and P lies on it. So AP is a line from A to that perpendicular bisector. The point P is also the center of the circle through A,C,D. So AP is the line joining A to the circumcenter. In a triangle, the line from a vertex to circumcenter is not generally perpendicular to the opposite side; it's the perpendicular bisector of the opposite side only if the triangle is isosceles at that vertex? Actually, in any triangle, the circumcenter is the intersection of perpendicular bisectors. So the line from vertex A to circumcenter P is the perpendicular bisector of side CD? No, that's not true: The perpendicular bisector of CD passes through P, but not necessarily through A. So AP is not necessarily perpendicular to CD unless A lies on that perpendicular bisector, which would mean A is equidistant to C and D, i.e., AC=AD. That is not generally true. So AP is not perpendicular to CD.

- But we found that the dot product (A-N)·v = -b(d+a+b)/2, and (A-M)·v = -a(d+a+b)/2. This suggests that the projection of A onto line AP is at a certain distance from N and M. Actually, (A-N)·v = |A-N| |v| cos(θ), where θ is angle between A-N and v. Since A-N is the vector from N to A, and v = P-A. Given the difficulty of synthetic reasoning, we can present a coordinate proof that is rigorous. The algebra, though messy, can be organized with clear steps. We'll need to show that the distance from O1 to line L equals R1.

We have derived coordinates for O1 and H and v. We can compute the distance from O1 to L using the formula for distance from a point to a line. We can also compute the radius R1 as distance from O1 to B. We can then show equality by simplifying using the relation y0^2 = ((a+b)^2-d^2)K/16. 

Let's attempt to compute d^2 = |(O1-H)×v|^2/|v|^2 and compare to R1^2.

We have O1-H = (x-x_P, y-y_H). We have expressions for these. Let's denote:

Δx = x - x_P = [ (a+b)d - 2(b-a)^2 ]/(4(b-a)).
Δy = y - y_H = d y0 [ 1/(d+a+b) - d/(2K) ] = d y0 * (2K - d(d+a+b))/(2K(d+a+b)).

Now compute N = Δx * dy - Δy * dx. We'll compute N^2 and |v|^2 R1^2. Since we have a lot of factors, we can try to simplify N by expressing everything in terms of a,b,d, and maybe using that y0^2 = ((a+b)^2-d^2)K/16.

Let's compute Δx and Δy more systematically.

First, note that (b-a)^2 = (a-b)^2. And we have d > b-a, so positive.

Compute Δx = ( (a+b)d - 2(b-a)^2 )/(4(b-a)). Let's denote T = (a+b)d - 2(b-a)^2. We'll keep.

Compute Δy = d y0 * (2K - d(d+a+b))/(2K(d+a+b)). Let's denote U = 2K - d(d+a+b). Then Δy = d y0 * U/(2K(d+a+b)).

Now dy = - (a+b) K (d+a+b)/(4d^2 y0). dx = (b-a)(d+a+b)/(2d).

So compute N = Δx * dy - Δy * dx = [T/(4(b-a))] * [ - (a+b) K (d+a+b)/(4d^2 y0) ] - [ d y0 U/(2K(d+a+b)) ] * [ (b-a)(d+a+b)/(2d) ] = - (a+b) K (d+a+b) T / (16 d^2 y0 (b-a)) - (b-a) y0 U / (4K). Because: d y0 * U/(2K(d+a+b)) * (b-a)(d+a+b)/(2d) = (b-a) y0 U/(4K). And then minus that: Actually N = Δx dy - Δy dx = (first term) - (second term). So N = - (a+b) K (d+a+b) T/(16 d^2 y0 (b-a)) - (b-a) y0 U/(4K). So N = - [ (a+b) K (d+a+b) T/(16 d^2 y0 (b-a)) + (b-a) y0 U/(4K) ].

Now we can combine into a single fraction. Multiply numerator and denominator by 16 d^2 y0 (b-a) K to get common denominator? Let's compute N = - (1/(16 d^2 y0 (b-a) K)) * [ (a+b) K^2 (d+a+b) T + 4 d^2 y0^2 (b-a)^2 U ]. Because:

First term: (a+b) K (d+a+b) T/(16 d^2 y0 (b-a)) = [ (a+b) K^2 (d+a+b) T ] / (16 d^2 y0 (b-a) K). Second term: (b-a) y0 U/(4K) = [4 d^2 y0^2 (b-a)^2 U] / (16 d^2 y0 (b-a) K). So sum inside = [ (a+b) K^2 (d+a+b) T + 4 d^2 y0^2 (b-a)^2 U ] / (16 d^2 y0 (b-a) K). Then N = - that sum. So

N = - [ (a+b) K^2 (d+a+b) T + 4 d^2 y0^2 (b-a)^2 U ] / (16 d^2 y0 (b-a) K).

Now, we want to compute |N|^2 / |v|^2 and compare to R1^2.

We have |v|^2 = 4ab (a+b+d)/(d^2 (a+b-d)). 

Also, R1^2 = (x - x0)^2 + (y + y0)^2. We have expressions for x-x0 and y+y0. But perhaps we can also compute R1^2 using the fact that O1 is the circumcenter of BEF, so it's the intersection of perpendicular bisectors. Now, we also have R1^2 = (x-x0)^2+(y+y0)^2. So we want to show that N^2 = |v|^2 R1^2.

Given the symmetry, maybe we can compute N in terms of T and U and then use the expressions for y0^2 to simplify. Also note that T = (a+b)d - 2(b-a)^2. And U = 2K - d(d+a+b). We can express these in terms of a,b,d. Also we have y0^2 = ((a+b)^2-d^2)K/16.

Let's compute the numerator of N: N_num = (a+b) K^2 (d+a+b) T + 4 d^2 y0^2 (b-a)^2 U. Substitute y0^2:

4 d^2 y0^2 (b-a)^2 U = 4 d^2 * ((a+b)^2-d^2)K/16 * (b-a)^2 U = d^2 K (a+b)^2 - d^2 K d^2? Actually: 4/16=1/4, so = (d^2/4) * K * ((a+b)^2-d^2) * (b-a)^2 U. So N_num = (a+b) K^2 (d+a+b) T + (d^2/4) K ((a+b)^2-d^2) (b-a)^2 U.

Thus N = - [ (a+b) K^2 (d+a+b) T + (d^2/4) K ((a+b)^2-d^2) (b-a)^2 U ] / (16 d^2 y0 (b-a) K) = - [ (a+b) K (d+a+b) T + (d^2/4) ((a+b)^2-d^2) (b-a)^2 U ] / (16 d^2 y0 (b-a) ). Because we canceled one K.

So N = - [ (a+b) K (d+a+b) T + (d^2/4) ((a+b)^2-d^2) (b-a)^2 U ] / (16 d^2 y0 (b-a) ). Write as:

N = -1/(16 d^2 y0 (b-a)) * [ (a+b) K (d+a+b) T + (d^2/4) ((a+b)^2-d^2) (b-a)^2 U ].

Now we also have |v|^2 = 4ab (a+b+d)/(d^2 (a+b-d)). So |v| = 2 sqrt(ab (a+b+d)/(d^2 (a+b-d))). And we need R1^2.

Let's compute R1^2. We have x-x0 = (a+b)(d^2+2(b-a)^2)/(4d(b-a)). And y+y0 = sqrt((a+b-d)(a+b+d)) (2 sqrt(K) - d^2)/(8 sqrt(K)). But we can also compute R1^2 in a similar manner.

Maybe we can find that N^2/|v|^2 simplifies to something like (x-x0)^2+(y+y0)^2. We can try to compute N in terms of these factors.

Given the time, I think we can produce a coordinate proof that is clean if we set up the coordinate system and then use algebraic manipulation with symmetric polynomials. We can use the fact that the expressions simplify using the identity for the common chord. Let's attempt to compute d^2 = |N|^2/|v|^2 and R1^2 and show equality by substituting the expressions we derived for x-x0 and y+y0.

We have:

R1^2 = (x - x0)^2 + (y + y0)^2.

We have:

x - x0 = (a+b)(d^2+2(b-a)^2)/(4d(b-a)).
y + y0 = sqrt((a+b-d)(a+b+d)) (2 sqrt(K) - d^2)/(8 sqrt(K)).

But maybe we can square and simplify.

Compute (y+y0)^2 = ((a+b)^2-d^2) K (2 sqrt(K) - d^2)^2/(64 K) = ((a+b)^2-d^2) (2 sqrt(K) - d^2)^2/64. Because K cancels: (K*(2√K - d^2)^2)/(64K) = (2√K - d^2)^2/64. So (y+y0)^2 = ((a+b)^2-d^2) (2√K - d^2)^2/64.

Thus R1^2 = (a+b)^2 (d^2+2(b-a)^2)^2/(16 d^2 (b-a)^2) + ((a+b)^2-d^2) (2√K - d^2)^2/64.

Now we need to compute N^2/|v|^2. N = -1/(16 d^2 y0 (b-a)) * [ (a+b) K (d+a+b) T + (d^2/4) ((a+b)^2-d^2) (b-a)^2 U ].

Thus N^2 = [ (a+b) K (d+a+b) T + (d^2/4) ((a+b)^2-d^2) (b-a)^2 U ]^2 / (256 d^4 y0^2 (b-a)^2).

Now |v|^2 = 4ab (a+b+d)/(d^2 (a+b-d)). So N^2/|v|^2 = [ (a+b) K (d+a+b) T + (d^2/4) ((a+b)^2-d^2) (b-a)^2 U ]^2 / (256 d^4 y0^2 (b-a)^2) * (d^2 (a+b-d))/(4ab (a+b+d)). So N^2/|v|^2 = [ (a+b) K (d+a+b) T + (d^2/4) ((a+b)^2-d^2) (b-a)^2 U ]^2 * d^2 (a+b-d) / (1024 d^4 y0^2 (b-a)^2 ab (a+b+d) ) = [ (a+b) K (d+a+b) T + (d^2/4) ((a+b)^2-d^2) (b-a)^2 U ]^2 * (a+b-d) / (1024 d^2 y0^2 (b-a)^2 ab (a+b+d) ).

Now we need to show this equals R1^2.

But we also have y0^2 = ((a+b)^2-d^2)K/16. So substitute: 1/y0^2 = 16/(K((a+b)^2-d^2)). Then

N^2/|v|^2 = [ (a+b) K (d+a+b) T + (d^2/4) ((a+b)^2-d^2) (b-a)^2 U ]^2 * (a+b-d) * 16/(K((a+b)^2-d^2)) / (1024 d^2 (b-a)^2 ab (a+b+d) ) = [ (a+b) K (d+a+b) T + (d^2/4) ((a+b)^2-d^2) (b-a)^2 U ]^2 * (a+b-d) * 16 / (1024 d^2 (b-a)^2 ab (a+b+d) K ((a+b)^2-d^2) ). Simplify 16/1024 = 1/64. So

N^2/|v|^2 = [ (a+b) K (d+a+b) T + (d^2/4) ((a+b)^2-d^2) (b-a)^2 U ]^2 * (a+b-d) / (64 d^2 (b-a)^2 ab (a+b+d) K ((a+b)^2-d^2) ).

Now we need to show this equals R1^2. This seems heavy.

Maybe we can find that the expression inside the square simplifies to something like (a+b) * something * (a+b-d) * (a+b+d) * (b-a)^2 * etc. Let's try to compute T and U in terms of a,b,d.

T = (a+b)d - 2(b-a)^2. Note that (b-a)^2 = (a-b)^2.

U = 2K - d(d+a+b). And K = d^2 - (a-b)^2. So U = 2(d^2 - (a-b)^2) - d(d+a+b) = 2d^2 - 2(a-b)^2 - d^2 - d(a+b) = d^2 - d(a+b) - 2(a-b)^2.

Thus T and U are related: T = (a+b)d - 2(b-a)^2 = (a+b)d - 2(a-b)^2. And U = d^2 - d(a+b) - 2(a-b)^2. So T + U = (a+b)d + d^2 - d(a+b) - 4(a-b)^2 = d^2 - 4(a-b)^2. Not obviously simpler.

Now also we have factors (a+b+d) and (a+b-d). So we can write:

Let S = a+b. Then T = S d - 2(a-b)^2. U = d^2 - S d - 2(a-b)^2.

Now the numerator of the square is: (a+b) K (d+a+b) T + (d^2/4) ((a+b)^2-d^2) (b-a)^2 U = S K (d+S) T + (d^2/4) (S^2 - d^2) (b-a)^2 U.

Now note that (b-a)^2 = (a-b)^2. And S^2 - d^2 = (S-d)(S+d). So we have:

N_num = S K (S+d) T + (d^2/4) (S-d)(S+d) (a-b)^2 U.

Factor (S+d): = (S+d)[ S K T + (d^2/4) (S-d) (a-b)^2 U ].

Subtract to get: -2 t_E L + t_E^2 |v|^2 = -2 t_F L + t_F^2 |v|^2 => 2L(t_F - t_E) = |v|^2(t_F^2 - t_E^2) => L = |v|^2(t_E+t_F)/2. And then we computed t_E = a(d+a+b)/|v|^2, t_F = b(d+a+b)/|v|^2, so t_E+t_F = (a+b)(d+a+b)/|v|^2. Thus L = |v|^2 * (a+b)(d+a+b)/(2|v|^2) = (a+b)(d+a+b)/2. So that L is the constant we derived, and it is the dot product (O1-A)·v? Actually we had L = (x-x0) dx + (y-y0) dy. Yes, because (O1-A) = (x-x0, y-y0) and v = (dx, dy). So L = (O1-A)·v.

Thus the condition that O1 is the circumcenter of BEF implies that (O1-A)·v = (a+b)(d+a+b)/2. This is a linear condition. Also, O1 must satisfy that the distances to B and E are equal, which gave us the equation for y. So we have two conditions that determine O1. Then we solved for x and y using these. In particular, we used the condition |O1-B|^2 = |O1-E|^2 to get an expression for y, and then used the linear condition to get x. That gave us x = x0 + (L - dy(y-y0))/dx. And then we simplified that to x = d/2 + (a+b)d/(4(b-a)). That was a computation that we did. But for numeric, that gives 1.875, while the actual O1 x-coordinate is 0.75. So there must be an error in that simplification.

Let's re-evaluate the computation of x from the linear condition. We have L = (x-x0) dx + (y-y0) dy = (a+b)(d+a+b)/2. So (x-x0) = (L - (y-y0) dy)/dx. So x = x0 + (L - (y-y0) dy)/dx.

Now we have expressions for x0, y, y0, dy, dx, L. Let's compute these for numeric using exact values.

Let's check: For the circumcenter, we have |O1-B|^2 = |O1-E|^2. That gave us an equation involving y and L. We used that to solve for y, but we also used that L is known. Wait, we used the equation from |O1-B|^2 = |O1-E|^2 to get y. That equation involved L and t_E and |v|^2. We solved for y, but we used L = (a+b)(d+a+b)/2. So that gives a specific y. Then we used the linear condition to get x. So the point (x,y) should satisfy both equations. But we computed distances and they were not equal. So either our computation of y from that equation is incorrect, or the equation itself is not correct. Let's re-derive the condition from |O1-B|^2 = |O1-E|^2.

We have:

|O1-B|^2 = (x-x0)^2 + (y+y0)^2.
|O1-E|^2 = (x-x_E)^2 + (y-y_E)^2.

But E = A + t_E v, so x_E = x0 + t_E dx, y_E = y0 + t_E dy.

Set equality:

(x-x0)^2 + (y+y0)^2 = (x - x0 - t_E dx)^2 + (y - y0 - t_E dy)^2.

Expand:

LHS: (x-x0)^2 + y^2 + 2y y0 + y0^2.
RHS: (x-x0)^2 - 2t_E dx (x-x0) + t_E^2 dx^2 + (y-y0)^2 - 2t_E dy (y-y0) + t_E^2 dy^2 = (x-x0)^2 + y^2 - 2y y0 + y0^2 - 2t_E (dx (x-x0) + dy (y-y0)) + t_E^2 (dx^2+dy^2). 

Cancel (x-x0)^2 and y^2 and y0^2:

2y y0 = -2y y0 - 2t_E (dx (x-x0) + dy (y-y0)) + t_E^2 |v|^2.

Thus 4y y0 = -2t_E (dx (x-x0) + dy (y-y0)) + t_E^2 |v|^2. So

2y y0 = - (t_E/2) (dx (x-x0) + dy (y-y0)) + (t_E^2 |v|^2)/4. (Equation 1)

Similarly, from |O1-B|^2 = |O1-F|^2 we get:

2y y0 = - (t_F/2) (dx (x-x0) + dy (y-y0)) + (t_F^2 |v|^2)/4. (Equation 2)

Now, we also have the condition from |O1-E|^2 = |O1-F|^2 (which is automatically satisfied if the other two hold, but we can use it to get a relation). However, we used the two equations to eliminate the linear form and got L = dx(x-x0)+dy(y-y0) = (a+b)(d+a+b)/2. Let's verify that step.

Subtract Equation 2 from Equation 1:

0 = - (t_E/2)L + (t_E^2|v|^2)/4 + (t_F/2)L - (t_F^2|v|^2)/4 = (L/2)(t_F - t_E) + (|v|^2/4)(t_E^2 - t_F^2) = (L/2)(t_F - t_E) - (|v|^2/4)(t_F - t_E)(t_F + t_E). So

0 = (t_F - t_E)[ L/2 - (|v|^2/4)(t_F + t_E) ].

Since t_F ≠ t_E, we get L/2 = (|v|^2/4)(t_F + t_E) => L = (|v|^2/2)(t_F + t_E). That's what we had.

Now, we also have from the definition of t_E: t_E = -2 (A-M)·v / |v|^2. But we already computed (A-M)·v = -a(d+a+b)/2, so t_E = a(d+a+b)/|v|^2. Similarly, t_F = b(d+a+b)/|v|^2. So t_E + t_F = (a+b)(d+a+b)/|v|^2. Then L = (|v|^2/2) * (a+b)(d+a+b)/|v|^2 = (a+b)(d+a+b)/2. So L is determined.

Now, we also need to use one of the equations to relate y and L. For instance, from Equation 1: 2y y0 = - (t_E/2) L + (t_E^2|v|^2)/4. So y = [ - (t_E/2)L + (t_E^2|v|^2)/4 ] / (2 y0) = [ - (t_E L)/2 + (t_E^2|v|^2)/4 ] / (2 y0) = [ - t_E L + (t_E^2|v|^2)/2 ] / (4 y0). Substitute t_E = a(d+a+b)/|v|^2, then:

But L = (a+b)(d+a+b)/2, so -aL + a^2(d+a+b)/2 = -a*(a+b)(d+a+b)/2 + a^2(d+a+b)/2 = (d+a+b)/2 * ( -a(a+b) + a^2 ) = (d+a+b)/2 * ( -a^2 - ab + a^2 ) = -ab(d+a+b)/2. So then y = [ (d+a+b)/|v|^2 * ( -ab(d+a+b)/2 ) ] / (4 y0) = [ -ab (d+a+b)^2/(2|v|^2) ] / (4 y0) = -ab (d+a+b)^2/(8 y0 |v|^2). That's what we had. So y = -ab(d+a+b)^2/(8 y0 |v|^2). Then half chord length = sqrt(r2^2 - (distance)^2)= sqrt(4 - 3.0625)= sqrt(0.9375)=0.9682458, consistent. So the chord length is indeed 1.93649.

Now why does the formula give 1.45235? Because the formula I gave is for the length of the common chord of two circles? Let's derive the formula correctly. The common chord is the line of points with equal power to both circles. The distance from M to the chord is x0 = (d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2)/(2d). The chord length is 2 sqrt(r1^2 - x0^2). So chord length squared = 4(r1^2 - x0^2) = 4r1^2 - 4x0^2. Now compute 4r1^2 - 4x0^2 = 4r1^2 - (d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2)^2/d^2. Multiply numerator and denominator: = [4r1^2 d^2 - (d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2)^2] / d^2. Now compute (d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2)^2 = (d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2)^2. So chord length squared = (4r1^2 d^2 - (d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2)^2) / d^2. Factor as difference of squares: = [ (2r1 d)^2 - (d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2)^2 ] / d^2 = [ (2r1 d - (d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2)) (2r1 d + (d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2)) ] / d^2. Now 2r1 d - (d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2) = -d^2 + 2r1 d - r1^2 + r2^2 = -(d^2 - 2r1 d + r1^2) + r2^2 = -(d - r1)^2 + r2^2 = r2^2 - (d - r1)^2. And 2r1 d + (d^2 + r1^2 - r2^2) = d^2 + 2r1 d + r1^2 - r2^2 = (d + r1)^2 - r2^2. So chord length squared = [ (r2^2 - (d - r1)^2) ((d + r1)^2 - r2^2) ] / d^2 = [ ( (r2 - (d - r1))(r2 + (d - r1)) ((d + r1) - r2)((d + r1) + r2) ] / d^2. But note that r2 + (d - r1) = d + r2 - r1. And (d + r1) - r2 = d + r1 - r2. So chord length squared = [ (r2 - d + r1)(d + r2 - r1) (d + r1 - r2)(d + r1 + r2) ] / d^2. Now note that r2 - d + r1 = (r1 + r2 - d). And d + r2 - r1 = (d + r2 - r1). And d + r1 - r2 = (d + r1 - r2). And d + r1 + r2 = (d + r1 + r2). So chord length squared = [ (r1 + r2 - d)(d + r2 - r1)(d + r1 - r2)(d + r1 + r2) ] / d^2. This is symmetric. For numeric: (r1+r2-d)=3-1.5=1.5, (d+r2-r1)=1.5+2-1=2.5, (d+r1-r2)=1.5+1-2=0.5, (d+r1+r2)=1.5+3=4.5, product = 1.5*2.5*0.5*4.5 = 1.5*2.5=3.75, 3.75*0.5=1.875, 1.875*4.5=8.4375. Then chord length = sqrt(8.4375)/1.5 = 2.9047/1.5 = 1.9365. Yes! So the correct formula is chord length = (1/d) * sqrt( (r1+r2-d)(d+r2-r1)(d+r1-r2)(d+r1+r2) ). That is the length of the common chord. So the formula I had earlier, (1/2) sqrt(((r1+r2)^2-d^2)(d^2-(r1-r2)^2)), is actually the length of the common chord? Let's compute that: ((r1+r2)^2-d^2)= (r1+r2-d)(r1+r2+d), and (d^2-(r1-r2)^2)= (d - (r1-r2))(d + (r1-r2)) = (d + r2 - r1)(d + r1 - r2). So product = (r1+r2-d)(r1+r2+d)(d+r2-r1)(d+r1-r2). Then sqrt of that is sqrt( (r1+r2-d)(d+r2-r1)(d+r1-r2)(r1+r2+d) ). Then half of that is (1/2) sqrt( (r1+r2-d)(d+r2-r1)(d+r1-r2)(r1+r2+d) ). This is not the same as (1/d) sqrt( (r1+r2-d)(d+r2-r1)(d+r1-r2)(d+r1+r2) ) unless d = r1+r2? So the correct formula for chord length is: AB = (1/d) sqrt( (r1+r2-d)(d+r2-r1)(d+r1-r2)(d+r1+r2) ). That is a known formula. So my earlier formula was wrong. The common chord length is given by: (1/d) * sqrt( ( (r1+r2)^2 - d^2 )( d^2 - (r1-r2)^2 ) )? Actually compute: sqrt( ((r1+r2)^2-d^2)(d^2-(r1-r2)^2) ) = sqrt( (r1+r2-d)(r1+r2+d)(d-r1+r2)(d+r1-r2) ) = sqrt( (r1+r2-d)(d+r2-r1)(d+r1-r2)(r1+r2+d) ). Then divide by d? That would give AB = sqrt( (r1+r2-d)(d+r2-r1)(d+r1-r2)(r1+r2+d) ) / d. But earlier we had chord length = (1/d) sqrt( (r1+r2-d)(d+r2-r1)(d+r1-r2)(d+r1+r2) ). Since r1+r2+d = d+r1+r2, it's the same. So the correct formula is AB = sqrt( (r1+r2-d)(d+r2-r1)(d+r1-r2)(d+r1+r2) ) / d. That is the length. So I had the wrong factor: it's not 1/2, it's 1/d. So the half-chord length squared is (1/(4d^2)) * product? Actually AB/2 = (1/(2d)) sqrt(product). So y0^2 = (1/(4d^2)) * (r1+r2-d)(d+r2-r1)(d+r1-r2)(d+r1+r2). So y0^2 = ((r1+r2-d)(d+r2-r1)(d+r1-r2)(d+r1+r2)) / (4d^2). For numeric: product = 1.5*2.5*0.5*4.5 = 8.4375, divided by 4*2.25=9, so y0^2=8.4375/9=0.9375, correct. So we have:

y0^2 = ((a+b-d)(d+b-a)(d+a-b)(d+a+b)) / (4d^2). That is the correct expression.

Thus our earlier expression for y0^2 = ((a+b)^2 - d^2)K/16 was wrong because we used a wrong factor. Let's derive that: (a+b)^2 - d^2 = (a+b-d)(a+b+d). And K = (d+a-b)(d+b-a). So product = (a+b-d)(a+b+d) * (d+a-b)(d+b-a) = (a+b-d)(d+b-a)(d+a-b)(a+b+d). So ((a+b)^2 - d^2)K = (a+b-d)(a+b+d) * (d+a-b)(d+b-a) = product. Then dividing by 16 gives product/16. So the correct expression is y0^2 = product/(4d^2) = product/(4d^2). So product = 4d^2 y0^2. Then ((a+b)^2 - d^2)K = product = 4d^2 y0^2. So y0^2 = ((a+b)^2 - d^2)K/(4d^2). That is the correct relation. So we had an extra factor of 4 in denominator? Actually we had y0^2 = ((a+b)^2-d^2)K/16. That is wrong; it should be / (4d^2). So correct: y0^2 = ((a+b)^2-d^2)K/(4d^2). For numeric: ((a+b)^2-d^2)=6.75, K=1.25, product=8.4375, divided by 4d^2=4*2.25=9, gives 0.9375, correct. So that's fixed.

Now we also have the expression for |v|^2 = AP^2 = ab (a+x0)(b+d-x0)/y0^2. And we have (a+x0)(b+d-x0) = K (a+b+d)^2/(4d^2). So |v|^2 = ab * [K (a+b+d)^2/(4d^2)] / y0^2 = ab K (a+b+d)^2/(4d^2 y0^2). Now substitute y0^2 = ((a+b)^2-d^2)K/(4d^2) => 1/y0^2 = 4d^2/(((a+b)^2-d^2)K). Then |v|^2 = ab K (a+b+d)^2/(4d^2) * (4d^2/(((a+b)^2-d^2)K)) = ab (a+b+d)^2/((a+b)^2-d^2). So |v|^2 = ab (a+b+d)^2/((a+b)^2-d^2). For numeric: a+b=3, (a+b)^2-d^2=9-2.25=6.75, (a+b+d)=4.5, square=20.25, ab=2, so 2*20.25/6.75=40.5/6.75=6, correct. So |v|^2 = ab (a+b+d)^2/((a+b)^2-d^2). Good.

Now we also have L = (a+b)(a+b+d)/2. So that is consistent.

Now we want to compute N^2/|v|^2 and R1^2.

We have N = dy(x-x_P) - dx(y-y_H). We can compute these using the simplified expressions we derived for x-x_P, y-y_H, dy, dx, and then simplify using y0^2 expression.

But perhaps we can find a more direct relationship: The point O1 is the circumcenter of BEF. Since B is the reflection of A across MN, and E,F lie on AP, there might be a relation that the circle (BEF) is the reflection of the circle through A and the inverses of something. Alternatively, we can use the fact that the line through H parallel to AP is the Simson line of point A with respect to triangle BEF if A lies on the circumcircle of BEF. But does A lie on circumcircle of BEF? Let's check numeric: A=(-0.25,0.9682), B=(-0.25,-0.9682), E=(0.875,-0.4841), F=(2,-1.9365). Compute distances from A to O1: O1=(0.75,-1.7428). dx=1, dy=2.710, distance= sqrt(1+7.34)=2.89. Radius R1=2.129, so A is not on circle. So not.

Maybe we can use the concept of the radical axis: The line through H parallel to AP is the polar of A with respect to circle (BEF). For a circle, the polar of a point A is the line such that if X is on the polar, then the cross ratio of the lines from A to the intersection points of any line through A with the circle is harmonic. The distance from the center to the polar is related to the power of A. Specifically, if A is at distance d from the center, and the radius is R, then the distance from the center to the polar is R^2/d. So if we can show that the distance from O1 to L is R1^2 / (distance from O1 to A)? Actually the polar of point A with respect to a circle with center O and radius R is the line perpendicular to OA at a distance R^2/OA from O. So if L is the polar of A, then the distance from O to L is R^2 / OA. But we have L is the line through H parallel to AP. So we need to show that L is the polar of A with respect to circle (BEF). That would mean that the distance from O1 to L is R1^2 / O1A. Then L is tangent to the circle if and only if the distance from O1 to L equals R1? Actually the polar of A is a line that does not necessarily touch the circle; it's the line that is the inverse of the point A. But if A lies on the circle, the polar is the tangent. If A is inside, the polar does not intersect the circle. In our numeric, we have L tangent to the circle, so the line L is actually the tangent at some point, but is it the polar of A? For the polar, the distance from O to L is R^2/d. For A, d = O1A = distance from O1 to A. We computed O1A = sqrt( (0.75+0.25)^2 + (-1.7428-0.9682)^2 ) = sqrt(1 + (-2.711)^2)= sqrt(1+7.35)=2.89. Then R^2/d = (2.129^2)/2.89 = 4.53/2.89=1.57, but the distance from O1 to L is R1=2.129? Actually we found d = distance from O1 to L = 2.129. That is not R^2/d. So L is not the polar of A. So that approach is not.

Given the complexity, I think it's better to produce a coordinate proof that is complete and rigorous. I'll set up the coordinate system, derive all points, compute the circumcenter O1 of BEF, compute the line L through H parallel to AP, and then show that the distance from O1 to L equals the radius R1. I'll simplify using algebraic identities, and I'll present the algebra in a clear, step-by-step manner. I'll try to reduce the expressions to manageable forms.

Let's proceed.

Set up:

Let M = (0,0), N = (d,0), with d>0. Let Ω: x^2+y^2 = a^2, Γ: (x-d)^2+y^2 = b^2, with a<b and circles intersect, so |a-b|<d<a+b. Define:

x0 = (d^2 + a^2 - b^2)/(2d). Then y0 = sqrt(a^2 - x0^2) > 0. Then A = (x0, y0), B = (x0, -y0). C = (-a, 0), D = (d+b, 0).

P is circumcenter of ACD. Compute:

x_P = (x_C + x_D)/2 = (-a + (d+b))/2 = (d+b-a)/2.
y_P = - x_P (x0+a)/y0. (Derived from PA=PC).

Now v = P - A = (x_P - x0, y_P - y0). Compute:

dx = x_P - x0 = (d+b-a)/2 - x0. Simplify: dx = (b-a)(d+a+b)/(2d). (Using x0 = (d^2+a^2-b^2)/(2d).)

dy = y_P - y0 = -x_P(x0+a)/y0 - y0 = -(x_P(x0+a)+y0^2)/y0. Simplify using x_P = (d+b-a)/2, x0+a = ((d+a)^2-b^2)/(2d). Then compute: x_P(x0+a) = ((d+b-a)(d+a-b)(d+a+b))/(4d). And y0^2 = a^2 - x0^2 = ((a+b)^2-d^2)(d+b-a)(d+a-b)/(4d^2)? Actually we have y0^2 = ((a+b-d)(d+b-a)(d+a-b)(d+a+b))/(4d^2). Then x_P(x0+a)+y0^2 =? Compute: x_P(x0+a) = (d+b-a)(d+a-b)(d+a+b)/(4d). And y0^2 = ((a+b-d)(d+b-a)(d+a-b)(d+a+b))/(4d^2). So sum = (d+a+b)(d+b-a)(d+a-b)/(4d) * [1 + (a+b-d)/(d)]? Actually factor (d+a+b)(d+b-a)(d+a-b)/(4d) =? Then plus y0^2 = (d+a+b)(d+b-a)(d+a-b)/(4d^2) * (a+b-d). So sum = (d+a+b)(d+b-a)(d+a-b)/(4d) * [1 + (a+b-d)/d] = (d+a+b)(d+b-a)(d+a-b)/(4d) * [ (d + a+b-d)/d ] = (d+a+b)(d+b-a)(d+a-b)/(4d) * ((a+b)/d) = (a+b)(d+a+b)(d+b-a)(d+a-b)/(4d^2). Then dy = - (sum)/y0 = - (a+b)(d+a+b)(d+b-a)(d+a-b)/(4d^2 y0). But note that (d+b-a)(d+a-b) = K. So dy = - (a+b)(d+a+b)K/(4d^2 y0). That is an expression.

Now we need t_E = a(d+a+b)/|v|^2, t_F = b(d+a+b)/|v|^2. And |v|^2 = ab (a+x0)(b+d-x0)/y0^2 = ab * (K (a+b+d)^2/(4d^2)) / y0^2 = ab K (a+b+d)^2/(4d^2 y0^2). But we have y0^2 = ((a+b-d)(d+b-a)(d+a-b)(d+a+b))/(4d^2) = ((a+b-d)K(d+a+b))/(4d^2). So 1/y0^2 = 4d^2/((a+b-d)K(d+a+b)). Then |v|^2 = ab K (a+b+d)^2/(4d^2) * (4d^2/((a+b-d)K(d+a+b))) = ab (a+b+d)^2/((a+b-d)(a+b+d)) = ab (a+b+d)/(a+b-d). So |v|^2 = ab (a+b+d)/(a+b-d). Good.

Now E = A + t_E v = (x0 + t_E dx, y0 + t_E dy). Similarly F.

Now compute H: orthocenter of triangle PMN. Since M=(0,0), N=(d,0), P=(x_P, y_P). The altitude from P to MN is vertical line x = x_P. The altitude from M to PN: PN vector = (d-x_P, -y_P). A direction perpendicular to PN is (y_P, d-x_P) (since dot((d-x_P, -y_P),(y_P, d-x_P)) = (d-x_P)y_P + (-y_P)(d-x_P)=0). So the altitude from M is line through origin with direction (y_P, d-x_P). Its equation: y = ((d-x_P)/y_P) x. Intersection with x = x_P gives y_H = ((d-x_P)/y_P) x_P. So H = (x_P, x_P(d-x_P)/y_P). We can compute x_P(d-x_P) =? x_P = (d+b-a)/2, so d-x_P = (d - (d+b-a)/2) = (2d - d - b + a)/2 = (d + a - b)/2. Then x_P(d-x_P) = ((d+b-a)(d+a-b))/4 = K/4? Because K = (d+b-a)(d+a-b). So x_P(d-x_P)=K/4. Then y_H = (K/4)/y_P = K/(4y_P). But y_P = -x_P(x0+a)/y0. So y_H = K/(4 * (-x_P(x0+a)/y0)) = - K y0/(4 x_P (x0+a)). And x_P (x0+a) =? x_P = (d+b-a)/2, x0+a = ((d+a)^2-b^2)/(2d) = (d+a-b)(d+a+b)/(2d). So product = (d+b-a)(d+a-b)(d+a+b)/(4d) = K (d+a+b)/(4d). Then y_H = - K y0 / (4 * (K (d+a+b)/(4d))) = - K y0 * (4d)/(4K(d+a+b)) = - d y0/(d+a+b). So H = (x_P, - d y0/(d+a+b)). Good.

Now we have all points.

Now we need to find the circumcenter O1 of triangle BEF.

We can use the fact that O1 is the intersection of perpendicular bisector of EF and perpendicular bisector of BE. Since EF is along v, its perpendicular bisector is the line perpendicular to v passing through the midpoint of EF. Let's denote:

Let M_EF = ( (x_E + x_F)/2, (y_E + y_F)/2 ) = ( x0 + (t_E+t_F)dx/2, y0 + (t_E+t_F)dy/2 ). Since t_E+t_F = (a+b)(d+a+b)/|v|^2? Actually t_E = aK'/|v|^2? Wait, t_E = a(d+a+b)/|v|^2, t_F = b(d+a+b)/|v|^2, so t_E+t_F = (a+b)(d+a+b)/|v|^2. And |v|^2 = ab (a+b+d)/(a+b-d). So t_E+t_F = (a+b)(a+b+d) * (a+b-d)/(ab(a+b+d)) = (a+b)(a+b-d)/(ab). So t_E+t_F = (a+b)(a+b-d)/(ab). That is a constant.

Now the perpendicular bisector of EF is the line through M_EF with direction perpendicular to v: say n = (dy, -dx) (since dot(dx,dy)·(dy,-dx)=0). So its equation: (x - M_EF_x) dy - (y - M_EF_y) dx = 0, i.e., dy x - dx y = dy M_EF_x - dx M_EF_y.

Now, also we have the condition that O1 is equidistant to B and E: |O1-B|^2 = |O1-E|^2. This gives a linear equation in x,y as we derived earlier: 2y y0 = - (t_E/2) L + (t_E^2|v|^2)/4, where L = dx(x-x0)+dy(y-y0). But we can use that to express y in terms of L. However, we already know that the circumcenter must satisfy that its projection onto line AP is at a specific distance from A. But we might be able to compute O1 directly using vector formulas.

Another approach: Since we have coordinates for B, E, F, we can compute the circumcenter by solving the system of equations:

(O1 - B)^2 = (O1 - E)^2 and (O1 - B)^2 = (O1 - F)^2. Subtract to get linear equations.

Let's do that.

Let O1 = (x,y). Then:

(1) (x-x0)^2 + (y+y0)^2 = (x - x_E)^2 + (y - y_E)^2.
(2) (x-x0)^2 + (y+y0)^2 = (x - x_F)^2 + (y - y_F)^2.

Expand (1):

(x-x0)^2 + y^2 + 2y y0 + y0^2 = (x - x_E)^2 + (y - y_E)^2 = x^2 - 2x x_E + x_E^2 + y^2 - 2y y_E + y_E^2.

Cancel x^2 and y^2:

(x-x0)^2 + 2y y0 + y0^2 = -2x x_E + x_E^2 -2y y_E + y_E^2.

Now, (x-x0)^2 = x^2 - 2x x0 + x0^2. So we have:

x^2 - 2x x0 + x0^2 + 2y y0 + y0^2 = -2x x_E + x_E^2 -2y y_E + y_E^2.

Rearrange: bring all terms to one side:

x^2 - 2x x0 + x0^2 + 2y y0 + y0^2 + 2x x_E - x_E^2 + 2y y_E - y_E^2 = 0.

=> x^2 + 2x(x_E - x0) + (x0^2 - x_E^2) + 2y(y0 + y_E) + (y0^2 - y_E^2) = 0.

But note that x_E = x0 + t_E dx, y_E = y0 + t_E dy. So x_E - x0 = t_E dx, and x0^2 - x_E^2 = (x0 - x_E)(x0 + x_E) = -t_E dx (2x0 + t_E dx). Similarly, y0^2 - y_E^2 = -t_E dy (2y0 + t_E dy). So the equation becomes:

x^2 + 2x t_E dx - t_E dx (2x0 + t_E dx) + 2y(y0 + y0 + t_E dy) - t_E dy (2y0 + t_E dy) = 0? Wait, careful: 2y(y0 + y_E) = 2y(y0 + y0 + t_E dy) = 2y(2y0 + t_E dy). And (y0^2 - y_E^2) = -t_E dy (2y0 + t_E dy). So the sum of the y-terms: 2y(2y0 + t_E dy) - t_E dy (2y0 + t_E dy) = (2y - t_E dy)(2y0 + t_E dy). So equation (1) becomes:

x^2 + 2x t_E dx - t_E dx (2x0 + t_E dx) + (2y - t_E dy)(2y0 + t_E dy) = 0.

Similarly, from equation (2) we get:

x^2 + 2x t_F dx - t_F dx (2x0 + t_F dx) + (2y - t_F dy)(2y0 + t_F dy) = 0.

Subtract the two to get a linear equation in x and y. Subtract (2) from (1):

2x(t_E - t_F)dx - (t_E dx (2x0 + t_E dx) - t_F dx (2x0 + t_F dx)) + (2y - t_E dy)(2y0 + t_E dy) - (2y - t_F dy)(2y0 + t_F dy) = 0.

But we can also use the fact that the circumcenter O1 must satisfy the condition that its projection onto AP is at a specific distance. Alternatively, we can compute O1 by using the formula for the circumcenter in barycentric coordinates relative to triangle BEF. That might be messy.

Given the time, I'll try to compute O1 using a different method: Use the fact that O1 is the circumcenter of BEF, so it is the intersection of the perpendicular bisectors. Since we have a simple expression for the perpendicular bisector of EF (a line), and we have a simple expression for the perpendicular bisector of BE (another line). We can compute O1 by solving these two linear equations. Let's derive the equation of the perpendicular bisector of BE.

B = (x0, -y0). E = (x0 + t_E dx, y0 + t_E dy). Midpoint M_BE = (x0 + (t_E/2) dx, (-y0 + y0 + t_E dy)/2) = (x0 + (t_E/2) dx, (t_E/2) dy). The slope of BE: (y_E - y_B)/(x_E - x_B) = (y0 + t_E dy + y0)/(x0 + t_E dx - x0) = (2y0 + t_E dy)/(t_E dx). So perpendicular slope = - (t_E dx)/(2y0 + t_E dy). So equation of perpendicular bisector: y - (t_E/2) dy = - (t_E dx)/(2y0 + t_E dy) * (x - x0 - (t_E/2) dx). This is linear.

Alternatively, we can write the condition |O1-B|^2 = |O1-E|^2 as a linear equation in x,y. Let's do that.

We have:

(x - x0)^2 + (y + y0)^2 = (x - x_E)^2 + (y - y_E)^2.

Expand: (x^2 - 2x x0 + x0^2) + (y^2 + 2y y0 + y0^2) = (x^2 - 2x x_E + x_E^2) + (y^2 - 2y y_E + y_E^2). Cancel x^2,y^2:

-2x x0 + x0^2 + 2y y0 + y0^2 = -2x x_E + x_E^2 - 2y y_E + y_E^2.

Bring terms: 2x(x_E - x0) + 2y(y0 + y_E) = x_E^2 - x0^2 + y_E^2 - y0^2.

Thus:

2x t_E dx + 2y(2y0 + t_E dy) = (x_E^2 - x0^2) + (y_E^2 - y0^2). But x_E^2 - x0^2 = (x_E - x0)(x_E + x0) = t_E dx (2x0 + t_E dx). And y_E^2 - y0^2 = t_E dy (2y0 + t_E dy). So the right side = t_E dx (2x0 + t_E dx) + t_E dy (2y0 + t_E dy) = t_E [ dx (2x0 + t_E dx) + dy (2y0 + t_E dy) ] = t_E [ 2(x0 dx + y0 dy) + t_E (dx^2+dy^2) ] = t_E [ 2(x0 dx + y0 dy) + t_E |v|^2 ].

Now, we know x0 dx + y0 dy = -a(d+a+b)/2 (from earlier). So 2(x0 dx + y0 dy) = -a(d+a+b). And t_E |v|^2 = a(d+a+b). So 2(x0 dx + y0 dy) + t_E |v|^2 = -a(d+a+b) + a(d+a+b) = 0. Thus the right side = 0. Therefore, the equation simplifies to:

2x t_E dx + 2y(2y0 + t_E dy) = 0 => divide 2: x t_E dx + y(2y0 + t_E dy) = 0. (Equation A)

Similarly, from |O1-B|^2 = |O1-F|^2 we get:

x t_F dx + y(2y0 + t_F dy) = 0. (Equation B)
